General variables[edit]

♠ RA ♣ Enrico Cioni ♥ EC contributed general description.

♠ Expert ♣ PRG ♥

♠ Peak Date ♣ ♥


Temporal bounds[edit]

♠ Duration ♣ 1580-1778 CE ♥ Justification for end date: Cook's first arrival in the archipelago (1778 at Kauai - he did not visit the Big Island until 1779).

♠ Degree of centralization ♣ unitary state: 1580-1630 CE; quasi-polity: 1631-1690 CE; loose: 1691-1710CE; unitary state: 1711-1752CE; quasi-polity: 1753-1755 CE; unitary state: 1756-1778 CE ♥ Overall, this period was characterized by an initial period of political integration, followed by a split into two polities, then a general move towards 'islandwide integration' again in the time leading up to Cook's arrival.[1] By about 1580 'Umi had ascended as ruler of the entire island.[2][3] In Kirch's reckoning, 'Umi was the first true king of the Big Island, as opposed to the mere chiefs who had governed the island until then. 'Umi did not fill a merely ceremonial role, as the previous chiefs had, but controlled the use of land, instituted a system of territorial administration and ahupua'a land parcels, intensified food production, specialized labour, and elaborated the religious system.[4] I think we can consider this a unitary state. 'According to Kamakau, 'Umi divided his kingdom' between two of his sons, Keli'iokaloa-a-'Umi and Keawenui-a-'Umi, thus splitting the island, but Keli'iokaloa defeated his brother in battle and 'became the king of a once again unified island'.[5] After the death of King Lonoikamakahiki c. 1630 'follows [a] phase of political fragmentation, when the leeward and windward regions came under the control of the Mahi and 'Ī lineages of Kohala and Hilo, respectively'.[6] Nominally, Keakealanikane became king of the island, but 'his reign was either ineffectual or simply not recognized by the district chiefs'.[7] After the death of Keakealaniwahine around 1690, her son Keawe-'ikekahi-ali'i-o-ka-moku 'established some degree of unity over the island, even though the district chiefs retained considerable power':[8] this sounds like 'loose' control. After Keawe-'ikekahi-ali'i-o-ka-moku's death, 'A strong and powerful kingship reasserted itself again ... with Alapa'inui' c. 1710.[9] Around 1752 CE, a warrior chief with a claim to the throne, Kalani'ōpu'u, rose up against Alapa'inui and 'gained control of his natal districts of Ka'ū and Puna, while Alapa'inui ruled the rest of the island', so the Big Island became a quasi-polity once again.[10] After Alapa'inui's death, Kalani'ōpu'u managed to defeat Alapa'inui's son Keawe'ōpala and gain control of the whole island - this must have happened around 1755 as Kirch tells us that Keawe'ōpala's reign began c. 1754 but 'was short'.[11] Kalani'ōpu'u 'devoted considerable attention to administration' and held onto power long enough to greet Captain James Cook off the windward coast of Maui in 1778.[12] (Dates are approximate here due to the oral-historical nature of the sources. I have used Table 3.1 in Kirch's How Chiefs Became Kings as a guide).[13]

♠ Language ♣ Hawaiian ♥

Map[edit]


General Description[edit]

Hawai'i, also known as the Big Island, is the largest island of the Hawaiian archipelago. Our 'Hawaii 3' refers to the period from 1580 to 1778 CE. 1580 is the approximate date of the formation of the first island-wide unitary kingdom, while 1778 is the date of first European contact ‒ the arrival of Captain Cook.[16][17]

Population and political organization

By about 1580, 'Umi had become the first true 'king' (ali'i nui) of the entire Big Island.[18] In contrast with the chiefs who came before him, he is said to have exerted greater control over land tenure, instituted a system of territorial administration based on ahupua'a land parcels, intensified food production, and elaborated the religious system.[19]
After 'Umi, the island alternated several times between being split into two or more smaller polities, and being united under one ruler.[20] In times of greater integration, the kingdom was divided into districts (moku) which were each under the control of a major chief called an ali'i-'ai-moku.[21] Within districts were territories called ahupua'a, ruled by chiefs called ali'i-'ai-ahupua'a. The more powerful of these ali'i held more than one ahupua'a.[22] Below the ahupua'a chiefs, konohiki land managers (often their junior relatives) oversaw the collection of tribute from the commoner farmers, known as maka'āinana.[23]
Overall, this period was characterized by a high-density but stable population settled in all ecological zones, a secondary intensification of the dryland field systems, and endemic conquest warfare.[24] The population of the entire Hawaiian archipelago by Cook's arrival was certainly very large, but there is a long-standing debate regarding exact numbers. Estimates range between 250,000 and 800,000.[25]

Social Scale[edit]

♠ Polity territory ♣ [7,000-12,000] ♥ To account for a range of polity sizes. 10,432- the entire Big Island “By the late seventeenth century, four main polities had emerged, focused on the main islands of Kaua’i, O’ahu, Maui, and Hawai’i, with the fought-over smaller islands being incorporated into one or another of the main units. However, the political dynamism of Hawai’i [the archipelago] in late prehistoric and early historic times emanated primarily from the two largest and youngest islands, Maui and Hawai’i….The Maui and Hawai’i chiefs coveted the generously endowed production systems based on irrigation that these western islands offered. Not long before Cook’s fateful visit in 1778-79, the Maui paramount Kahekili expanded his polity to encompass all of the islands to the west and was engaged in a fierce succession of wars with his arch-rival Kalani’ōpu’u of Hawai’i. After the fateful encounter with the West, Kalani’ōpu’u’s successor—the famous Kamehameha I—made shrewd use of Western arms to incorporate the entire archipelago under his hegemony.”[26]

♠ Polity Population ♣ [37,500-200,000] ♥ Estimate calculated using a range of [150,000-800,000] inhabitants for the whole archipelago divided into 4 polities. The 150,000-200,000 figure is implausible considering that all men, women, and children in the entire archipelago may only have numbered 250,000-300,000 at this time [27]. However, if higher estimates of the archipelago’s population (400,000, possibly more than 800,000 [28]) are correct, perhaps the 1794 fleet really did contain 150,000-200,000 men.<br\ “By the late seventeenth century, four main polities had emerged, focused on the main islands of Kaua’i, O’ahu, Maui, and Hawai’i, with the fought-over smaller islands being incorporated into one or another of the main units. However, the political dynamism of Hawai’i [the archipelago] in late prehistoric and early historic times emanated primarily from the two largest and youngest islands, Maui and Hawai’i….The Maui and Hawai’i chiefs coveted the generously endowed production systems based on irrigation that these western islands offered. Not long before Cook’s fateful visit in 1778-79, the Maui paramount Kahekili expanded his polity to encompass all of the islands to the west and was engaged in a fierce succession of wars with his arch-rival Kalani’ōpu’u of Hawai’i. After the fateful encounter with the West, Kalani’ōpu’u’s successor—the famous Kamehameha I—made shrewd use of Western arms to incorporate the entire archipelago under his hegemony.”[29]

Hierarchical Complexity[edit]

♠ Settlement hierarchy ♣ 2 ♥
1. Royal/chiefly centre
2. Dispersed households
"Hawai'i lacked anything approaching urban centers, and although there were substantial differences in population density corresponding to an uneven topographic distribution of soil and hydrologic resources (see discussion that follows), the general trend was of dispersed households, each occupying and farming its own adjacent plots. Moreover, while the commoners were sedentary on their lands, the ali'i were known to move about in relation to available food stocks. This peripatetic pattern of chiefly movement is well described, and underlies the metaphor of the chief as a “shark who travels on the land” (He manōholo ̒āina ke ali'i; Pukui 1983:87: Proverb 799). Nonetheless, there were distinct chiefly and royal centers, marked by concentrations of larger residences adjacent to temples of the main state cults of Kū and Lono. [...] Surrounding the king's own extensive household compound were the residential courts of several principal ali'i and advisors, the houses of warriors, and the main Hale o Lono or temple to the god in whose name the annual tribute was collected. While this settlement plan has certain innovations reflecting Western contact (notably the gun drilling (p.51) field and the shipyard), in most respects it was probably typical of royal courtly centers in the late precontact era." [30]
♠ Administrative levels ♣ 4 ♥ This number equal to the levels in the provincial administration, plus the ali'i nui.
1. Ali'i nui
"At the apex of the polity sat the king, the ali'i nui or 'great ali'i,' [...]. The al'i nui ruled over the entire mokupuni [island], assisted by various administrative aides." [31]

__Central administration__

2. Kalaimoku
"The kālaimoku was charged with advising the king on all secular affairs, including war. Among his chief duties was to oversee the royal storehouses 'in which to collect food, fish, tapa [barkcloth], malo [loincloths], pa-u [female skirts], and all sorts of goods' (Malo 1951:195). Only the kālaimoku had the regular privilege of holding secret meetings with the king, and he controlled the access of other al'i to royal audiences." [32]
2. Kahuna nui
The kahuna nui "carried the responsibilities for the king's religious duties and looked after his temples and main gods." [33]

__Provincial administration__

2. Ali'i-'ai-moku
"The districts (moku) into which the kingdom was divided were each under the control of a major chief of high rank, called the ali'i-'ai-moku. The operative term 'ai in this compound term has the core meaning of both 'food' and 'eat' but with metaphoric extensions connoting to 'consume,' 'grasp,' or 'hold onto' (Pukui and Elbert 1986:9). Thus the figurative extension of 'ai includes 'to rule, reign, or enjoy the privileges and exercise the responsibilities of rule.' The term ali'i-'ai-moku might thus be simply translated 'ruler of the moku,' but as in many Hawaiian expressions there are layers of kaona, 'hidden meanings', folded in. He is as well the chief who 'eats' the district (recall the metaphor of the chief as land shark), and literally 'eats from' its productions." [34]
3. Ali'i-'ai-ahupua'a
"[T]he more numerous ahupua'a territories were apportioned to chiefs who were called the ali'i-'ai-ahupua'a, the chiefs who “ate” the ahupua'a. Low-ranked chiefs might hold just a single, marginal land unit, but more powerful and higher-ranked ali'i frequently held more than one ahupua'a." [35]
4. Konohiki
"The three tiered hierarchy of land rulers, beginning with the ali'i nui who had the power to reallocate lands to the ali'i-'ai-moku and ali'i-'ai-ahupua'a under him, did not extend down below the level of the largely self-sufficient ahupua'a territories. Rather, the administration of the ahupua'a, including its various ̒ili subdivisions, was put into the hands of a konohiki, a resident “land manager” who acted on behalf of the ali'i-'ai-ahupua'a. Konohiki were, in fact, often lower-ranked members of the al'i class (such as kaukau ali'i), frequently junior collaterals of the ahupua'a chiefs themselves." [36]
♠ Religious levels ♣ 2 ♥ Though there were many different types of ritual specialists in pre-contact Hawaii [37], only one kind, the kahuna pule, was involved with state affairs. Other types include healing experts, sorcerers, and prophets [38].
1. Kahuna nui
The kahuna nui was the most important of the kahuna pule (see below), as he "carried the responsibilities for the king's religious duties and looked after his temples and main gods." [39]
2. Kahuna pule
"The priests who officiated at temples controlled by the king and major chiefs were the kahuna pule. These were subdivided into a number of specific orders or cults, especially those pertaining to Kū and Lono (mo'o Kū and mo'o Lono). These priests were drawn from high-ranking elite families, typically of papa rank (in which the person's mother comes from one of the three highest ranks)." [40]

♠ Military levels ♣ [3-4] ♥ There does not seem to have been a separate military hierarchy, so this estimate is based on a modified version of the administrative hierarchy, in which the kalaimoku is given greater weight because of his role as adviser in times of war.

1. Ali'i nui
"At the apex of the polity sat the king, the ali'i nui or 'great ali'i,' [...]. The al'i nui ruled over the entire mokupuni [island], assisted by various administrative aides." [41]
2. Kalaimoku
"The kālaimoku was charged with advising the king on all secular affairs, including war. Among his chief duties was to oversee the royal storehouses 'in which to collect food, fish, tapa [barkcloth], malo [loincloths], pa-u [female skirts], and all sorts of goods' (Malo 1951:195). Only the kālaimoku had the regular privilege of holding secret meetings with the king, and he controlled the access of other al'i to royal audiences." [42]
3. Ali'i-'ai-moku
"The districts (moku) into which the kingdom was divided were each under the control of a major chief of high rank, called the ali'i-'ai-moku. The operative term 'ai in this compound term has the core meaning of both 'food' and 'eat' but with metaphoric extensions connoting to 'consume,' 'grasp,' or 'hold onto' (Pukui and Elbert 1986:9). Thus the figurative extension of 'ai includes 'to rule, reign, or enjoy the privileges and exercise the responsibilities of rule.' The term ali'i-'ai-moku might thus be simply translated 'ruler of the moku,' but as in many Hawaiian expressions there are layers of kaona, 'hidden meanings', folded in. He is as well the chief who 'eats' the district (recall the metaphor of the chief as land shark), and literally 'eats from' its productions." [43]
3. Ali'i-'ai-ahupua'a
"[T]he more numerous ahupua'a territories were apportioned to chiefs who were called the ali'i-'ai-ahupua'a, the chiefs who “ate” the ahupua'a. Low-ranked chiefs might hold just a single, marginal land unit, but more powerful and higher-ranked ali'i frequently held more than one ahupua'a." [44]

Professions[edit]

♠ Occupational complexity ♣ ♥

♠ Professional military officers ♣ absent ♥ Chiefs and stewards were the military officers[45], so there does not appear to have been any specifically *military* officers.

♠ Professional soldiers ♣ present ♥ There were full-time elite warriors[46]. Kalani’ōpu’u of the Big Island had a large standing army[47]

♠ Professional priesthood ♣ present ♥ There were full-time priests[48][49]

Bureaucracy characteristics[edit]

♠ Full-time bureaucrats ♣ absent ♥ Konohiki formed a “primitive bureaucracy” according to Sahlins[50]. There was also an “incipent bureaucracy”[51] composed of specialists in memorizing genealogies, traditions, and other information.

♠ Source of support ♣ ♥

♠ Examination system ♣ inferred absent ♥ inferred from discussion of sources of development/introduction in later periods

♠ Specialized government buildings ♣ inferred absent ♥ inferred from discussion of sources of development/introduction in later periods

Law[edit]

♠ Formal legal code ♣ absent ♥ The first formal legal code dates to 1827: "These three laws were: first, against murder, 'the one who commits murder here shall die, by being hung'; second, against theft, 'the one who steals shall be put in irons'; third, against adultery, for which the penalty was imprisonment in irons. Three other proposed laws, against rum selling, prostitution, and gambling, were drawn up, to be explained and taught to the people before they should be adopted. It was agreed that the chiefs should meet six months later to continue their consultation upon the subject. The three laws adopted and the three proposed were printed together on one sheet, which bears the date December 8, 1827. On December 14, the people were assembled in a coconut grove near the fort; the three enacted laws were formally proclaimed, and the king, Kaahumanu, and Boki exhorted the people, both native and foreign, to obey the three laws which had been adopted and to give attention to the three which were not yet enacted." [52]

♠ Judges ♣ absent ♥ There were no specialized judges - konohiki and ali'i adjudicated disputes such as conflicts over water rights[53].

♠ Courts ♣ inferred absent ♥ inferred from discussion of sources of development/introduction in later periods

♠ Professional Lawyers ♣ inferred absent ♥ inferred from discussion of sources of development/introduction in later periods

Specialized Buildings: polity owned[edit]

♠ Communal buildings ♣ ♥

♠ Special purpose house ♣ ♥

♠ Utilitarian public buildings ♣ present ♥ Irrigation infrastructure was extensive, including water-diversion walls for dryland agriculture[54] and pondfields for taro[55]. Irrigation was utilized in Kohala and Hāmākua valleys on the Big Island[56].

♠ irrigation systems ♣ present ♥ Irrigation infrastructure was extensive, including water-diversion walls for dryland agriculture[57] and pondfields for taro[58]. Irrigation was utilized in Kohala and Hāmākua valleys on the Big Island[59]. These irrigation systems were certainly “public” as only a chief commanded the labor necessary to construct such large works.
♠ drinking water supply systems ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ markets ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ food storage sites ♣ {absent; present} ♥ High status individuals (chiefs, etc.) had houses for the storage of provisions as part of their household clusters[60]. Food storage sheds were called hale papa’a. All scholars agree that these provisions were intended for redistribution, but it is unclear to whom. Sahlins[61] implies that the food was redistributed to the people, including commoners. But Kirch[62] states that the food was redistributed almost entirely to other chiefs, lesser chiefs, retainers, etc., with only token amounts, at most, going to commoners. He also states that food storage was difficult given the climate and kinds of crops that Hawaiians cultivated, so chiefs had to physically travel to different areas of their chiefdoms in order to exact tribute, rather than being able to store all of the tribute in a central location[63]. Meanwhile, Valeri states that none of the tribute was redistributed to the commoners[64]. Sahlins and Kirch seem to agree that chiefs would sometimes, or at least were expected to, provide food to commoners in the event of famine[65][66].

♠ Symbolic building ♣ present ♥ Heiau (temples) “evinced the power and authority of ruling chiefs, and served as constant reminders of the role in the life of the community of an extensive pantheon” [67]. The heiau at Hōnaunau contained the bones of 23 kings and chiefs in wicker caskets; it was a site of “great sanctity”[68].

♠ Entertainment buildings ♣ present ♥ Some royal centers had hōlua slides, which were tracks, made of stone and earth, running down hillsides, on which players (of high status) could slide down on sleds as part of the competitive game called ‘hōlua’[69].

♠ Knowledge/information buildings ♣ inferred absent ♥ inferred from discussion of sources of development/introduction in later periods

Transport infrastructure[edit]

♠ Roads ♣ present ♥ There were trails, and these could possibly be called “roads” because sections of them were made of stone for easier travel (e.g. over sharp igneous rock) [70].
♠ Bridges ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Canals ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Ports ♣ inferred absent ♥ There were a few canoe-mooring holes in the South Point of the Big Island[71], but it is unclear who built them, and these probably do not constitute ports in any case.

The most impressive/costly building(s)[edit]

♠ height ♣ ♥
♠ extent ♣ ♥ [in m (the horizontal dimension along the longest axis)]
♠ cost ♣ ♥
Thousands worked on the site for about a year, including all but one supremely taboo chief, and many human sacrifices were required[72]. A human chain of 10-20,000 people was used to transport stones[73] [State-owned]
-The “Great Wall” at Hōnaunau had an average height of 3.7 m, width of 5.2 meters, and length of 304.8 meters[74], for a total volume 5,864 m^3 - the largest listed dimensions that I have found in the literature for any structure on the island. It is, however, unclear if it was still standing in its former glory by the time period of this polity (1778-1810).
-Kailua has King Kamehameha’s residence, called Kamakahonu, and also the luakini temple of Ahu’ena.
-At Kawaihae Bay, Kamehameha built a large luakini called Pu’ukoholā in 1791[75], probably the largest and most impressive heiau on the Big Island [76]. It was completed in 1791 and measured 224x100feet (~68.3m by 30.5m): It is described as follows: “a massive terraced and walled hilltop platform built of mortarless, waterworn lava rocks and boulders. Measuring about 224 by 100 feet, it contained walls on each end and the landward side. The side toward the sea remained open. Three narrow, terraced steps down the hillside to the west enabled the interior to be seen from the sea.”[77]. I code this as the most impressive building.

♠ Mnemonic devices ♣ ♥
♠ Nonwritten records ♣ present ♥ "What the Hawaiian kings and their incipient bureaucracy did have was a highly elaborated oral-aural culture, with specialists whose job it was to memorize genealogies, traditions, and important information of all kinds." [84]
♠ Written records ♣ absent ♥ "The lack of a writing system is also noteworthy, although Hawai'i is not the only archaic state with this deficiency; the Inka similarly lacked written texts." [85]

Fortifications

♠ Settlements in a defensive position ♣ present ♥ "defenders more commonly established a fortress site known as a pali (cliff) or pā kauau (war enclosure), a “natural or artificial fortress, where they leave their wives and children, and to which they fled if vanquished in the field.” One kind of fortress was the point of a narrow, steep-sided ridge that had been made somewhat defensible by digging deep trenches".[130]
♠ Wooden palisades ♣ inferred present ♥ "The Hawaiians generally did not build fortifications, but non-combatants could find sacred sanctuary in places of refuge known as pu'uhonua." Pg 4. [131] "A wooden palisade was the case at Kamehameha I's compound at Pakaka".[132]

[136]. Nevertheless, there does appear to evidence for some stone walls, but I'm not sure if they are used in warfare. The “Great Wall” at Hōnaunau, built around 1600 CE, was over 300m long, 3m high and 5m wide[137][138]. Lapakahi also had a “Great Wall”, which was built between about 1450 and 1500 CE[139].

Moreover, if there had been such a “long wall”, it is difficult to imagine that it would not have left any trace in the archaeological, historical, or oral-history record.

Cultural distance[edit]

♠ Wars within same ethnic group ♣ present ♥ Wars were within and between different Hawaiian polities.

♠ Wars involving groups with different dialects ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Hawaiian has different dialects, such as the Niihau dialect, but I'm not aware of specific evidence of warfare among speakers of different dialects.

♠ Wars involving groups with different languages ♣ absent ♥ Hawaiian polities only had contact with other Hawaiian polities.

♠ Wars involving groups with different language families ♣ absent ♥ "Despite oral narratives of an earlier period of two-way voyages between Hawai`i and an ancestral land called ``Kahiki, for example, the Hawaiians had not been in contact with other Polynesian groups for at least several centuries prior to Cook's arrival in 1778." Pg 85. [150]

♠ Wars between different religious sects ♣ suspected unknown ♥

♠ Wars between different world religions ♣ absent ♥ World religions were not present.

♠ Wars of world religion against tribal religion ♣ absent ♥ World religions were not present.

♠ Wars on Urbanism-Literacy frontier ♣ absent ♥ There was no urbanism-literacy distinction between Hawaiian groups.

Intensity of warfare[edit]

♠ Annexation ♣ present ♥ " The three largest islands - Hawai'i, Maui, and O'ahu - were the most politically active in the formation of aggressive multidistrict confederacies, the conquest of other islands, and (along with Kaua`i) the annexation of islands and districts." Pg 22[151]

♠ Looting ♣ present ♥ "A common strategy by the late eighteenth century, evidently designed to weaken an enemy kingdom's ability to wage war, was the wide-ranging demolition of its means of production. Invading forces destroyed agricultural fields, fishpond walls, irrigation systems, livestock, and houses" Pg. 35[152]

♠ Sack ♣ present ♥ "A common strategy by the late eighteenth century, evidently designed to weaken an enemy kingdom's ability to wage war, was the wide-ranging demolition of its means of production. Invading forces destroyed agricultural fields, fishpond walls, irrigation systems, livestock, and houses" Pg. 35[153]

♠ Rape ♣ suspected unknown ♥

♠ Deportation ♣ present ♥ "A common strategy by the late eighteenth century, evidently designed to weaken an enemy kingdom's ability to wage war, was the wide-ranging demolition of its means of production. Invading forces destroyed agricultural fields, fishpond walls, irrigation systems, livestock, and houses" Pg. 35[154].

♠ Expulsion ♣ inferred present ♥ "In the mid-eighteenth century, struggle over resources, according to Tikopia traditional accounts, had far more serious consequences, referred to by Firth as the ex- termination of Nga Ravenga and the expulsion of Nga Faea. Through details, such as minor supernatural fourishes, differ in various versions, in broad outline the stories were accepted as historical accounts of actual men and women, some of whose names appear in genealogies of Tikopia men who passed on the accounts to Firth in 1929. By genealogical count, assuming 20-year generations, the first of these is an account that dates from around 1740." Pg 161. [155]. I have used "inferred" present because this example of expulsion does not come from Big Island Hawaii but another island and there is no mention that this strategy is "common" (unlike other strategies listed above).

♠ Enslavement ♣ inferred present ♥ "A third small underclass consisted of the unfortunates known as kauwā,a term translated as “outcast, pariah, slave, untouchable, menial; a caste which lived apart and was drawn on for human sacrifices”" Pg 11 [156].

♠ Imprisonment ♣ inferred absent ♥ Not mentioned in book that outlines warfare strategies. [157].

Archaeological proxies:

♠ Skeletal evidence of violence ♣ suspected unknown ♥ To what period does any archaeological evidence for this date?: "pouo.manu. n. House post; post designating place of human sacrifice, said to be named for an ancient deity."[158]

"Matthew Spriggs suggests that there may have been Tahitian influence on Hawaiian development in the period when voyages between the two were reported in traditional accounts, by modern reckoning, between 1100 and 1400 CE: 'In traditional histories this is the 'migration period' when two-way voyaging took place between Tahiti and Hawii bringing new chiefs and new ideas, in particular a new religious system involving human sacrifice and ceremonies in walled temples from which the common people were excluded.' ... Archaeological evidence for such a connection is slight, and other scholars doubt the accuracy of the traditional account concerning Tahitian influence."[159]

Skeletal data on Hawaiian groups, such as for cranial trauma, is available from the Smithsonian Human Skeleton Database. Sites used by one study dated 1853-1930.[160] "To help gauge the magnitude and severity of violence in Rapa Nui's history, comparable data reported in the literature or in the Smithsonian Skeletal Biology Progam's Human Skeleton Database are presented in Tables 13.8, 13.9, and 13.10. While not large in number, Maori and Hawaiian collections are included to enable some comparison with other Polynesians. Native groups from North America (California, the central and Northern Great Plains, New Mexico, and Tennessee) and South America (Perus) with varying patterns of violence or warfare were also selected. ... Based on the cranial vault, the Rapanui have more fractures than observed in other Polynesian groups. Hawaiian crania in the Smithsonian collection, for example, had few vault fractures (2.6%), none of which was lethal (Table 13.8)."[161]

♠ Mass burials ♣ suspected unknown ♥ No data yet found in the literature.

♠ Settlement destruction ♣ suspected unknown ♥ No data yet found in the literature.

♠ Pictorial evidence of warfare ♣ suspected unknown ♥ No data yet found in the literature.

List of Wars[edit]

Most wars were for the purposes of raiding before c. 1570 CE; thereafter, they were more for conquest and annexation.[162]

Ritual variables[edit]

♠ RA ♣ PRG; Enrico Cioni ♥

♠ Expert ♣ ♥

Largest scale collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ kapu Makahiki sensu stricto ♥ "On the evening of the twenty-third day [of the year and of the Makahiki cycle], after the god is decorated, everyone gets dressed in his best. Nobles and commoners chant aloud prayer called kauwō (K. Kamaku 1919-20, 41; cf. PE, 129). Pigs are cooked in the oven for the men and dogs for the women. Then the celebration begins. Everywhere cries of people drunk on 'awa can be heard, as well as blasphemous, reviling, and cursing songs (kūamuamu). Everyone enjoys himself. Halfway through the night, nobles and commoners together go to bathe in the ocean. The Makahiki gods are placed in the sand on the beach, and everywhere fires are lighted. This bathing, called hi'uwai, lasts until dawn (K. Kamaku 1919-20, 41; Malo MS, 149). "This night, particularly the bathing period, is the occasion for a sexual orgy and verbal obscenities. As Kepelino writes, 'The effect of holding his hi'uwai at night was that the crowd were [sic] excited as if with rum by the beauty of the ornaments, the splendor of the whale-tooth pendants, bracelets of sea-shells, feather wreaths and the fragrance of different tapa garments; one person was attracted to another and the result was by no means good. [1932, 96, cf. 193-95]' "At dawn of the twenty-fourth day all the people leave the sea, which, from that moment, just like any water, belongs to the god Lono (see below) and is forbidden to man. "For four days one only has to eat well and have a good time; working and even cooking are forbidden (Fornander 1916-20, 6:204). This period is the kapu Makahiki senseu stricto. K. Kamakau lists all the prohibitions in force during this time: 'And the deity [Lono Makua--V.V.] had decreed his law that man was prohibited not [sic] to kill; war was prohibited and no [sic] fighting, the ocean was prohibited, not a canoe was to sail; the kapa block was prohibited and no cloth was to be beaten; the drum was prohibited to be beaten; the horn was prohibited to be blown; the land prohibited to be loosened {e kapu ka aina, aole, e hemo, "the land is taboo, without exception"]; [...].' Moreover, human sacrifices, sacrifices of pigs, bananas, or coconuts--that is, those consecrated in the temples in the so-called haipule rites--are forbidden (Malo 1951, 141; MS, 145-146; Kamakau 1964, 19). This is the time for 'games', 'play', and 'jokes'--all designated by the word pa'ani--for all but the king, the priests, and the kahu akua (Malo MS, 146; cf. Kepelino 1932, 94), who should not take part in them." [163] "Naturally, the hi'uwai is also a rite of passage. The seawater is supposed to 'purify' the bathers of all ritual impurities contracted during the year just ended (Beckwith (1932, 194). [...] "Scatological or obscene songs are improvised that reveal sins and faults and provoke laughter, which is a form of castigation (Ii 1963, 72-73). Thus people 'purify' one another through play instead of purifying themselves, as normally happens, through sacrifice. "Undoubtedly this is also the time when hundreds (Vancouver 1801, 5: 128-129), or even thousands (Stewart 1839, 95), of people participate in dances that enact mythological and historical episodes (Ellis 1917, 59, 74, 78-80; Beechey 1831, 2: 105-6; Stewart 1839, 145). These dances often have an erotic character, especially since it is permitted, and even prescribed, to go on from words to deeds: the dancers, whatever their sex, cannot refuse the sexual advances of the spectators they have aroused (cf. Pukui [1942] in Barrére, Pukui, and Kelly 1980, 78).]" [164]
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ yearly ♥ This ritual takes place for four days, starting on the evening of the twenty-third day of the year (and of the Makahiki ritual cycle) [165].
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ yearly ♥ This ritual takes place for four days, starting on the evening of the twenty-third day of the year (and of the Makahiki ritual cycle) [166].
♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ not applicable ♥ This ritual takes place for four days, starting on the evening of the twenty-third day of the year (and of the Makahiki ritual cycle) [167].
♠ Duration ♣ 96 ♥ hours. "For four days one only has to eat well and have a good time" [168].
♠ Typical size of participating group ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Source does not provide absolute numbers for the ritual generally, but the celebrations did include a dance in which, according to missionary sources, the performers numbered in the hundreds [169] or the audience numbered in the thousands [170].
♠ Inclusiveness (ritual specialist) ♣ elites ♥ Because this ritual is part of the state cult, it seems reasonable to infer that it was supervised by the king and the priests, though they did not participate directly in the revelry that was part of the celebration [171].
♠ Inclusiveness (participants) ♣ majority ♥ It seems that only kings, priests and kahu akua (keepers of the gods) were not allowed to participate in the revelry [172].
♠ Inclusiveness (audience) ♣ not applicable ♥ The ritual appears to have been public, taking place mostly in the open--there is no indication, in the sources, that any group of people was forbidden from observing the events, though it should be noted that kings, priests and kahu akua (keepers of the gods) were forbidden from participating [173]. Thomas Cressy: It was a blank code due to RA discussions on how the audience actively participated. I have coded as 'not applicable' based on what RAs decided.
Dysphoric elements
♠ fasting ♣ absent ♥ This was a time of feasting [174].
♠ vigil/sleep deprivation ♣ present ♥ The inaugural celebrations, on the evening of the twenty-third day of the year, last all the way through the night [175].
♠ fear ♣ inferred absent ♥ Inferred from the fact that this is not mentioned by the source, as well as by the euphoric nature of the ritual, and the fact that violence of all kinds was forbidden [176].
♠ humiliation ♣ absent ♥ This was a rare time in which commoners were allowed to be blasphemous towards gods and chiefs, and when they bathe together with the elite [177]. Moreover, people's sins and flaws were exposed through scatological or obscene songs meant to provoke laughter, not through more severe forms of punishment [178].
♠ disgust ♣ inferred absent ♥ Inferred from the fact that this is not mentioned by the source, as well as by the euphoric nature of the ritual [179].
♠ poisoning/dysphoria-causing drugs ♣ inferred absent ♥ Inferred from the fact that this is not mentioned by the source, as well as by the euphoric nature of the ritual [180].
♠ pain ♣ absent ♥ Violence of all kinds was forbidden [181].
♠ mutilation ♣ absent ♥ Violence of all kinds was forbidden [182].
♠ risk of death ♣ absent ♥ Violence of all kinds was forbidden [183].

Most widespread collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ daily offering ♥ "Shrines where offerings were made daily to Lono and to the ancestral deities ('aumākua) were a part of every household. These were frequently incorporated within the men's house or mua, as noted by Kamakau: 'The heiau ipu-o-Lono constantly maintained by the populace was the hale mua, the men's eating house, which every household had' (1976: 133). The ipu was a gourd container, in which offerings of food and fish were placed." [207] "At the level of the household, the hale mua is both the main house and the first to be built (Malo 1951, 28; Kamakau in Thrum 1910, 57; cf. Andrews 1922, 98). As the 'front part' of the group, the hale mua is also the place where guests are received and business is transacted (Judd 1975, 21, 24; Kamakau 1961, 212). Here one also enters into relation with another aspect of the 'outside,' of the world that transcends the household: the gods. Their images are put above an altar (kuahu) (Malo 1951, 87), where daily offerings are set, especially at the beginning of each meal. Like any temple, the mua is strictly forbidden to women (cf. Handy 1972, 316)." [208] NOTE: Haipule temple rites used to be coded as most frequent ritual, on the grounds that they involved the elites: the codebook still says that the the "official cult" is the "set of collective ritual practices that are most closely associated with legitimation of the power structure (including elites, if any)". However, the haipule temple rites only occurred on a weekly basis, and, following discussions with the ritual editor, it has been decided that the "official cult" actually includes any ritual that is "mainstream" within the polity, and that distinguishes one polity from the next (within reason), regardless of whether it is performed by elites or commoners. "It was in the mua, starting about age 5 years, that boys traditionally learned by daily example how to practice masculine skills and to become men (op. cit. 9)." [209] This suggests that the daily offering was one of many rituals that strengthened bonds between Hawaiian males.

Most widespread collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ daily offering ♥ "Shrines where offerings were made daily to Lono and to the ancestral deities ('aumākua) were a part of every household. These were frequently incorporated within the men's house or mua, as noted by Kamakau: 'The heiau ipu-o-Lono constantly maintained by the populace was the hale mua, the men's eating house, which every household had' (1976: 133). The ipu was a gourd container, in which offerings of food and fish were placed." [264] "At the level of the household, the hale mua is both the main house and the first to be built (Malo 1951, 28; Kamakau in Thrum 1910, 57; cf. Andrews 1922, 98). As the 'front part' of the group, the hale mua is also the place where guests are received and business is transacted (Judd 1975, 21, 24; Kamakau 1961, 212). Here one also enters into relation with another aspect of the 'outside,' of the world that transcends the household: the gods. Their images are put above an altar (kuahu) (Malo 1951, 87), where daily offerings are set, especially at the beginning of each meal. Like any temple, the mua is strictly forbidden to women (cf. Handy 1972, 316)." [265] NOTE: Haipule temple rites used to be coded as most frequent ritual, on the grounds that they involved the elites: the codebook still says that the the "official cult" is the "set of collective ritual practices that are most closely associated with legitimation of the power structure (including elites, if any)". However, the haipule temple rites only occurred on a weekly basis, and, following discussions with the ritual editor, it has been decided that the "official cult" actually includes any ritual that is "mainstream" within the polity, and that distinguishes one polity from the next (within reason), regardless of whether it is performed by elites or commoners. "It was in the mua, starting about age 5 years, that boys traditionally learned by daily example how to practice masculine skills and to become men (op. cit. 9)." [266] This suggests that the daily offering was one of many rituals that strengthened bonds between Hawaiian males.

Their images are put above an altar (kuahu) (Malo 1951, 87), where daily offerings are set, especially at the beginning of each meal." [267] Thomas Cressy: It was agreed that there is not enough evidence to code the presence of specialists.

Most euphoric collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ kapu Makahiki sensu stricto ♥ "On the evening of the twenty-third day [of the year and of the Makahiki cycle], after the god is decorated, everyone gets dressed in his best. Nobles and commoners chant aloud prayer called kauwō (K. Kamaku 1919-20, 41; cf. PE, 129). Pigs are cooked in the oven for the men and dogs for the women. Then the celebration begins. Everywhere cries of people drunk on 'awa can be heard, as well as blasphemous, reviling, and cursing songs (kūamuamu). Everyone enjoys himself. Halfway through the night, nobles and commoners together go to bathe in the ocean. The Makahiki gods are placed in the sand on the beach, and everywhere fires are lighted. This bathing, called hi'uwai, lasts until dawn (K. Kamaku 1919-20, 41; Malo MS, 149). "This night, particularly the bathing period, is the occasion for a sexual orgy and verbal obscenities. As Kepelino writes, 'The effect of holding his hi'uwai at night was that the crowd were [sic] excited as if with rum by the beauty of the ornaments, the splendor of the whale-tooth pendants, bracelets of sea-shells, feather wreaths and the fragrance of different tapa garments; one person was attracted to another and the result was by no means good. [1932, 96, cf. 193-95]' "At dawn of the twenty-fourth day all the people leave the sea, which, from that moment, just like any water, belongs to the god Lono (see below) and is forbidden to man. "For four days one only has to eat well and have a good time; working and even cooking are forbidden (Fornander 1916-20, 6:204). This period is the kapu Makahiki senseu stricto. K. Kamakau lists all the prohibitions in force during this time: 'And the deity [Lono Makua--V.V.] had decreed his law that man was prohibited not [sic] to kill; war was prohibited and no [sic] fighting, the ocean was prohibited, not a canoe was to sail; the kapa block was prohibited and no cloth was to be beaten; the drum was prohibited to be beaten; the horn was prohibited to be blown; the land prohibited to be loosened {e kapu ka aina, aole, e hemo, "the land is taboo, without exception"]; [...].' Moreover, human sacrifices, sacrifices of pigs, bananas, or coconuts--that is, those consecrated in the temples in the so-called haipule rites--are forbidden (Malo 1951, 141; MS, 145-146; Kamakau 1964, 19). This is the time for 'games', 'play', and 'jokes'--all designated by the word pa'ani--for all but the king, the priests, and the kahu akua (Malo MS, 146; cf. Kepelino 1932, 94), who should not take part in them." [321] "Naturally, the hi'uwai is also a rite of passage. The seawater is supposed to 'purify' the bathers of all ritual impurities contracted during the year just ended (Beckwith (1932, 194). [...] "Scatological or obscene songs are improvised that reveal sins and faults and provoke laughter, which is a form of castigation (Ii 1963, 72-73). Thus people 'purify' one another through play instead of purifying themselves, as normally happens, through sacrifice. "Undoubtedly this is also the time when hundreds (Vancouver 1801, 5: 128-129), or even thousands (Stewart 1839, 95), of people participate in dances that enact mythological and historical episodes (Ellis 1917, 59, 74, 78-80; Beechey 1831, 2: 105-6; Stewart 1839, 145). These dances often have an erotic character, especially since it is permitted, and even prescribed, to go on from words to deeds: the dancers, whatever their sex, cannot refuse the sexual advances of the spectators they have aroused (cf. Pukui [1942] in Barrére, Pukui, and Kelly 1980, 78).]" [322]
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ yearly ♥ This ritual takes place for four days, starting on the evening of the twenty-third day of the year (and of the Makahiki ritual cycle) [323].
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ yearly ♥ This ritual takes place for four days, starting on the evening of the twenty-third day of the year (and of the Makahiki ritual cycle) [324].
♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ not applicable ♥ This ritual takes place for four days, starting on the evening of the twenty-third day of the year (and of the Makahiki ritual cycle) [325].
♠ Duration ♣ 96 ♥ hours. "For four days one only has to eat well and have a good time" [326].
♠ Typical size of participating group ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Source does not provide absolute numbers for the ritual generally, but the celebrations did include a dance in which, according to missionary sources, the performers numbered in the hundreds [327] or the audience numbered in the thousands [328].
♠ Inclusiveness (ritual specialist) ♣ elites ♥ Because this ritual is part of the state cult, it seems reasonable to infer that it was supervised by the king and the priests, though they did not participate directly in the revelry that was part of the celebration [329].
♠ Inclusiveness (participants) ♣ majority ♥ It seems that only kings, priests and kahu akua (keepers of the gods) were not allowed to participate in the revelry [330].
♠ Inclusiveness (audience) ♣ not applicable ♥ The ritual appears to have been public, taking place mostly in the open--there is no indication, in the sources, that any group of people was forbidden from observing the events, though it should be noted that kings, priests and kahu akua (keepers of the gods) were forbidden from participating [331]. Thomas Cressy: It was a blank code due to RA discussions on how the audience actively participated. I have coded as 'not applicable' based on what RAs decided.

♠ humiliation ♣ absent ♥ This was a rare time in which commoners were allowed to be blasphemous towards gods and chiefs, and when they bathe together with the elite [335]. Moreover, people's sins and flaws were exposed through scatological or obscene songs meant to provoke laughter, not through more severe forms of punishment [336].

♠ other ♣ ♥

Most dysphoric collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

Another candidate for “most dysphoric collective ritual” is the actions that follow the death of a king, before the new king has taken the throne[365]. This involves self-mutilation as well as violence towards others. But I do not consider this to be the most dysphoric collective ritual for two reason: 1) According to Valeri it is hardly a ritual at all, since it is extremely chaotic and follows no particular script, 2) While there are significant dysphoric elements (grief, self-mutilation, violence, fear, probably rape, etc.) there are also euphoric elements (debauchery, extreme sexual license, flatterning of hierarchies, intoxication, etc.). - PRG

♠ Name ♣ hono ♥ "After the Hoowilimoo rites were over, the ritual of the dreaded hono ceremony was observed. This began the following day or on the second day. On that day, all the chiefs and men went bathing to rid themselves of the odor and grease of the pigs that they had eaten shortly before and, perhaps, in order to be purified. There were three or four pigs to be cooked that day for those who were to take part in the ceremony.
"After bathing, the participants in the hono were seated. The few who participated did so only in the expectation of receiving some pork from the three or four pigs cooked especially for the ceremony. The men sat crosslegged in rows in line with each of the images that stood from one end to the other before the 'anu'u tower, each man erect, one behind the other. Both elbows rested on the upper part of the thighs, the left wrist under the fight one. The participants waited to be told to 'put up the hands, up above.' Whoever wished to could raise both hands, but that was optional. Those who raised only the right hand rested the left one on the right knee, assuming as humble a posture as he saw fit. It was forbidden to shift one's position or move more than his arm while the kahuna of the hono uttered his prayers, which lasted a whole hour. When one of the company felt his upraised arms growing tired, he lowered them slowly to rest upon his thighs. But none dared to raise his head, lest he dies.
"In this ceremony, a person who kept both his hands upraised until the time was up made a decided impression. One who held the thumb of his upraised right hand erect was called a 'catcher of flies' (po'i nalo). When the ceremony was over, it was common to hear someone who had participated boast overt the steadiness of his upraised arms and his immobility, even when some thing he dreaded, such as a lizard or a rat, climbed onto his body." [366]
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ yearly ♥ This ritual was part of the ritual cycle dedicated to the god Kū, which occurred yearly [367].
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ yearly ♥ This ritual was part of the ritual cycle dedicated to the god Kū, which occurred yearly [368].
♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ suspected unknown ♥ This ritual was part of the ritual cycle dedicated to the god Kū, which occurred yearly [369].
♠ Duration ♣ 1 ♥ hour. The properly dysphoric section of the ritual took one hour: "It was forbidden to shift one's position or move more than his arm while the kahuna of the hono uttered his prayers, which lasted a whole hour." [370]
♠ Typical size of participating group ♣ suspected unknown ♥ The sources do not provide absolute numbers [371].
♠ Inclusiveness (ritual specialist) ♣ elites ♥ Priests officiating at temples belong to the nobility [372].
♠ Inclusiveness (participants) ♣ substantial minority ♥ This is not particularly clear, but it would seem that the ritual involved "chiefs and men" [373].
♠ Inclusiveness (audience) ♣ suspected unknown ♥ (Giuseppe Innamorato: Discussed with RA, unsure about any textual reference on the existence of an audience, further research needed.. There is some confusion in the sources, but it would seem that any man was allowed to watch this ritual: "Witnessed from special seating places outside of the immediate temple walls by assembled males, these terrifying rites reinforced the power of the king." [374].
