Limits on Power of the Chief Executive[edit]

Legal (formal) limits[edit]

♠ Executive power is separate or independent from military ♣ absent ♥ The ali'i nui of Hawai'i wielded military power and led troops into battles on land and at sea. For instance, Alapa'inui (early 18th century CE) is described as assembling large forces and war canoes and attacking the ali'i of other islands.[405] The kings' military power was reinforced symbolically in their association with the war god Kū.[406] There are many discussions in the literature of 'traditional', 'late precontact' or 'contact-period' Hawaiian society that speak in synchronic terms without providing fixed dates for individual sociopolitical developments. Where I have been unable to find exact dates for the practices and institutions mentioned, I have interpreted 'traditional Hawaiian society' as referring to the period from 'Umi's accession c. 1580 CE to the death of Kamehameha in 1819 CE (at the end of the succeeding polity, USKameh). Some justification: Kirch tells us that the distinctive 'economic, social, and political structures' present in Hawaii by the time of European contact had 'emerged out of an earlier, more typical Polynesian chiefdom society within the previous two or three centuries',[407] and he treats 'Umi's reign as heralding a qualitatively different set of social and political arrangements on the Big Island. The death of Kamehameha in 1819 'effectively marked the end of the ancien régime.'[408]
♠ Executive power is separate or independent from legislative ♣ absent ♥ There was no independent legislative body in Hawai'i before the 19th century,[409][410] and the system of laws was inextricably bound up with the kapu system of 'taboos'. Hommon comments: 'A legal system is an important feature of the state as defined here, but distinguishing law from custom in an emerging primary state such as Hawai'i may be difficult ... In my view, though the roots of kapu can be traced back millennia, in emerging Hawaiian states the concept took on additional significance as the basis for laws, that is, rules promulgated and enforced by a central government'.[411] Kānāwai, edicts, could have a royal origin,[412] as when Kamehameha I issued the 'Law of the Splintered Paddle' in the succeeding polity.[413] There are many discussions in the literature of 'traditional', 'late precontact' or 'contact-period' Hawaiian society that speak in synchronic terms without providing fixed dates for individual sociopolitical developments. Where I have been unable to find exact dates for the practices and institutions mentioned, I have interpreted 'traditional Hawaiian society' as referring to the period from 'Umi's accession c. 1580 CE to the death of Kamehameha in 1819 CE (at the end of the succeeding polity, USKameh). Some justification: Kirch tells us that the distinctive 'economic, social, and political structures' present in Hawaii by the time of European contact had 'emerged out of an earlier, more typical Polynesian chiefdom society within the previous two or three centuries',[414] and he treats 'Umi's reign as heralding a qualitatively different set of social and political arrangements on the Big Island. The death of Kamehameha in 1819 'effectively marked the end of the ancien régime.'[415]
♠ Executive power is separate or independent from judiciary ♣ absent ♥ 'The government of the Hawaiian monarchy operated as a patrimonial system until constitutional change in 1887 restricted the power of the king to control executive, legislative, and judicial institutions. Ministers, governors, senior officials, legislators, and judges owed their positions to royal favor and influence, thus continuing for Hawaiian and foreign "service gentry" the practices of the pre-1840 kingdom by valuing loyalty higher than efficiency or selection by competition'.[416] JR: there are many discussions in the literature of 'traditional', 'late precontact' or 'contact-period' Hawaiian society that speak in synchronic terms without providing fixed dates for individual sociopolitical developments. Where I have been unable to find exact dates for the practices and institutions mentioned, I have interpreted 'traditional Hawaiian society' as referring to the period from 'Umi's accession c. 1580 CE to the death of Kamehameha in 1819 CE (at the end of the succeeding polity, USKameh). Some justification: Kirch tells us that the distinctive 'economic, social, and political structures' present in Hawaii by the time of European contact had 'emerged out of an earlier, more typical Polynesian chiefdom society within the previous two or three centuries',[417] and he treats 'Umi's reign as heralding a qualitatively different set of social and political arrangements on the Big Island. The death of Kamehameha in 1819 'effectively marked the end of the ancien régime.'[418]

Legal (formal) freedoms[edit]

♠ Right to petition executive ♣ present ♥ Minerbi explains the Hawaiian concept of the pu'uhonua, an 'inviolable' place of refuge where kapu-breakers or those fleeing war could find safety. These were physical spaces (walled enclosures), but the king's person also served as a pu'uhonua: 'The king was called pu'uhonua because one could run to him and be saved by him. His lap and his very person were pu'uhonua.'[419] In the succeeding polity, USKameh, Kamehameha's wife Ka'ahumanu was pu'uhonua and 'Any condemned person could be saved if [she] said the word'.[420]
♠ Critique of executive by government ♣ inferred absent ♥ In a discussion of 'heterarchy' in the late precontact and early contact-period Hawaiian state, Hommon notes that the government included a 'council of chiefs', and that the ali'i nui 'often sought the counsel of experienced chiefs ... before making important policy decisions, a wise course considering the fact that chiefs of old and influential district families decided to secede from kingdoms with some frequency'.[421] However, there is no mention here of a formal right to criticize the king's actions.
♠ Critique of executive by non-government ♣ inferred absent ♥ In the traditional Hawaiian system of governance, which endured until the death of Kamehameha I in the succeeding polity, the ali'i nui had formally absolute power.[422] Not even the highest-ranking chiefs appear to have had a legally enshrined right to criticize the executive,[423] so I think we can infer that non-governmental groups would not have had this right either.

Power distributed[edit]

♠ Sanction of executive by government ♣ inferred absent ♥ In a discussion of 'heterarchy' in the late precontact and early contact-period Hawaiian state, Hommon notes that the government included a 'council of chiefs', and that the ali'i nui 'often sought the counsel of experienced chiefs ... before making important policy decisions, a wise course considering the fact that chiefs of old and influential district families decided to secede from kingdoms with some frequency'.[424] However, the power exercised by the chiefs here stems from the threat of secession from the polity rather than any legal right to impose sanctions. I have not come across any suggestion in the sources that members of government had this right.
♠ Sanction of executive by non-government ♣ inferred absent ♥ I have not come across any suggestion in the sources that the general population (commoners) had a legal right to sanction the ali'i nui, and it seems very unlikely given that the ruler was considered to be a god[425] and that it was obligatory for commoners to lie face down on the ground 'in the presence of the highest-ranking chiefs and king'.[426] Violent commoner rebellions did occur,[427] but this cannot be considered a formal legal right.
♠ Constraint on executive by government ♣ inferred absent ♥ In a discussion of 'heterarchy' in the traditional Hawaiian state, Hommon notes that the government included a 'council of chiefs', and that the ali'i nui 'often sought the counsel of experienced chiefs ... before making important policy decisions, a wise course considering the fact that chiefs of old and influential district families decided to secede from kingdoms with some frequency'.[428] However, the power exercised by the chiefs here stems from the threat of secession from the polity rather than any legal right to constrain the executive's decisions. I have not come across any suggestion in the sources that members of government had this right.
♠ Constraint on executive by non-government ♣ inferred absent ♥ I have not come across any suggestion in the sources that the general population (commoners) had a legal right to constrain the actions of the ali'i nui, and it seems very unlikely given that the ruler was considered to be a god[429] and that it was obligatory for commoners to lie face down on the ground 'in the presence of the highest-ranking chiefs and king'.[430] Violent commoner rebellions did occur,[431] but this cannot be considered a formal legal right.
♠ Impeachment ♣ inferred absent ♥ I have found no mention of impeachment (whether formal or informal) in the sources. Hommon comments: 'The traditional accounts suggest that chiefly rivalry for active rulership tended to be resolved (often ending a war of succession) in one of two ways: either territory of the polity was partitioned, usually with multidistrict units awarded to each claimant ... or one claimant usurped the power of the other by eliminating him in a coup d'état'.[432]

Customary (informal) checks on executive[edit]

♠ Informal critique of executive ♣ [absent; present]: 1580-1610 CE; inferred present: 1611-1630 CE; [present; absent]: 1630-1777 CE ♥ If the traditions surrounding Kanaloa-kua'ana, one of three ali'i nui of the Big Island from c. 1610 to 1630 CE,[433] are to be believed, we can infer that there was informal criticism of the executive by religious agents and government officials. Apparently, Kanaloa-kua'ana's 'priests and counselors' goaded him into waging war against his half-brother 'Umiokalani by urinating in his presence, then saying: 'Your priests, counselors, and people have clear urines because they drink copiously of water. The urine of the chiefs, priests, and counselors of a wealthy chief is yellow through drinking 'awa and eating rich foods'.[434] More generally, the kālaimoku, chief councillor, 'was charged with advising the king on all secular affairs, including war'.[435] Perhaps this close advisor could also informally express disapproval of the king's actions?
♠ Informal sanction of executive ♣ ♥
♠ Informal constraint on executive ♣ inferred present ♥ 'As Kepelino makes clear in his own account of "the government," the Hawaiian system was highly decentralized, or distributed. While the king "was the supreme head," whose power was most clearly indicated by his ability to redistribute the moku and ahupua'a land divisions to the chiefs under his rule, the daily management of these hierarchically nested lands rested largely in the hands of the konohiki.'[436] It seems as though the king's power operated within certain boundaries, circumscribed by the power of priestly and secular officials in the royal court.
♠ Informal impeachment ♣ inferred absent ♥ I have found no mention of impeachment (whether formal or informal) in the sources. Hommon comments: 'The traditional accounts suggest that chiefly rivalry for active rulership tended to be resolved (often ending a war of succession) in one of two ways: either territory of the polity was partitioned, usually with multidistrict units awarded to each claimant ... or one claimant usurped the power of the other by eliminating him in a coup d'état'.[437]

Limits on executive power imposed by religious agents[edit]

♠ Critique of executive by religious agents ♣ inferred present: 1610-1630 CE ♥ If the traditions surrounding Kanaloa-kua'ana, one of three ali'i nui of the Big Island from c. 1610 to 1630 CE,[438] are to be believed, we can infer that there was informal criticism of the executive by religious agents and government officials. Apparently, Kanaloa-kua'ana's 'priests and counselors' goaded him into waging war against his half-brother 'Umiokalani by urinating in his presence, then saying: 'Your priests, counselors, and people have clear urines because they drink copiously of water. The urine of the chiefs, priests, and counselors of a wealthy chief is yellow through drinking 'awa and eating rich foods'.[439]
♠ Sanction of executive by religious agents ♣ ♥
♠ Constraint on executive by religious agents ♣ inferred present ♥ In later pre-contact Hawaii, the religious specialists known as kahuna constituted 'a formal class of priests, parallel to and competing with, the class of chiefs'.[440] According to Goldman, the highest-status priests were 'members of the royal line, in some respects more sacred than the ali'i, and with direct access to the gods'.[441] When 'chiefs acted against the high priests ... the gods smote the land with drought and famine'.[442] The threat of such divine retribution for kings who opposed the priesthood must have served as a constraint on the executive's actions.
♠ Constraint on executive by religious institutions ♣ present ♥ For four lunar months every year, a period known as the Makahiki, 'the cult of Kū was in abeyance and warfare was forbidden'.[443] Warfare and conquest were vital components of Hawaiian kingship - 'Kū, god of war [was] perforce the principal god of the king'[444] - so the institution of the Makahiki can be seen as a ritual constraint on the king's ability to make war on his rivals.
♠ Impeachment by religious agents ♣ inferred absent ♥ I have found no mention of impeachment (whether formal or informal) in the sources. Hommon comments: 'The traditional accounts suggest that chiefly rivalry for active rulership tended to be resolved (often ending a war of succession) in one of two ways: either territory of the polity was partitioned, usually with multidistrict units awarded to each claimant ... or one claimant usurped the power of the other by eliminating him in a coup d'état'.[445]
♠ Subordination of the chief executive to a religious agent ♣ absent ♥ By this point in Hawaiian history, the institution of divine kingship had developed.[446] As Valeri put it, 'the predicates of the king are identical to the predicates of the divine ... He unifies the divine in relation to the human; he is the perfect human because he instantiates all the properties of the divine'.[447]
♠ Motivation by belief in supernatural agent ♣ present ♥ Although the king was himself divine, he also devoted resources towards building temples and making sacrifices for various gods. Kirch says that 'when he goes to war, the king offers a human sacrifice at the luakini temple of Kū, but at other times he consecrates temples to the agricultural gods Lono and Kāne'.[448]

Mechanisms of Power Transfer (from one head of state to the next)[edit]

♠ Customary mechanism ♣ present ♥ 'Ideally the office of king was inherited by the eldest son of the previous ruler, but political and genealogical factors sometimes intervened'.[449] Birth order was an important factor, but alongside this, a complex system of rules for determining succession had developed: 'seniority had become embedded in a distinctive pattern of rank distinctions governed largely by marriage. The form of marriage which modified the otherwise absolute character of seniority had become most important among the highest ranks of chiefs for whom finer discriminations had become imperative. Brother-sister marriage produced offspring of the highest rank; marriages between children sired with her by each of the two husbands of a chiefess, and then marriages between classificatory siblings produced offspring of second and of third rank respectively'.[450]
♠ Legal mechanism ♣ inferred present ♥ 'Ideally the office of king was inherited by the eldest son of the previous ruler, but political and genealogical factors sometimes intervened'.[451] Birth order was an important factor, but alongside this, a complex system of rules for determining succession had developed: 'seniority had become embedded in a distinctive pattern of rank distinctions governed largely by marriage. The form of marriage which modified the otherwise absolute character of seniority had become most important among the highest ranks of chiefs for whom finer discriminations had become imperative. Brother-sister marriage produced offspring of the highest rank; marriages between children sired with her by each of the two husbands of a chiefess, and then marriages between classificatory siblings produced offspring of second and of third rank respectively'.[452] Hawai'i had no formal law code during this period, and 'distinguishing law from custom in an emerging primary state such as Hawai'i may be difficult'.[453] However, I have coded this 'inferred present' because the process of determining the heir to the throne had become formalized, with professional genealogists (kūauhau) some of the 'most respected of experts at court'.[454]
♠ Number of power transitions ♣ 11 ♥ 0.'Umi-a-Līloa (c. 1580-90 CE).[455][456] 1. Keli'iokāloa (c. 1590 CE?).[457] Inherited the kingship as son of 'Umi, with 'direct control of Kona, Kohala and Hāmākua districts'.[458] 2. Keawenui a 'Umi (c. 1590-1610 CE).[459][460] 3a. Kaikilaninui-ali'i-wahine-o-Puna (Kaikilaninui) (c. 1610-30 CE).[461][462] There are differing accounts of the succession after Keawenui, but in Hommon's interpretation there was a 'triarchy'.[463] Keawenui may have conferred the kingship simultaneously on three ali'i: Kaikilaninui, granddaughter of Keli'iokāloa, and her two husbands (Keawenui's sons by different women), Lonoikamakahiki and Kanaloakua'ana.[464] 3b. Lonoikamakahiki (c. 1610-30 CE).[465][466] There are differing accounts of the succession after Keawenui, but in Hommon's interpretation there was a 'triarchy'.[467] Keawenui may have conferred the kingship simultaneously on three ali'i: Kaikilaninui, granddaughter of Keli'iokāloa, and her two husbands (Keawenui's sons by different women), Lonoikamakahiki and Kanaloakua'ana.[468] 3c. Kanaloakua'ana (c. 1610-30 CE).[469][470] There are differing accounts of the succession after Keawenui, but in Hommon's interpretation there was a 'triarchy'.[471] Keawenui may have conferred the kingship simultaneously on three ali'i: Kaikilaninui, granddaughter of Keli'iokāloa, and her two husbands (Keawenui's sons by different women), Lonoikamakahiki and Kanaloakua'ana.[472] 4. Keakea-lani-kāne (mid-17th century CE).[473][474] After Lonoikamakahiki's death, he inherited the kingship as son of Kaikilaninui-ali'i-wahine-o-puna (called Kaikilani-Ali'i-Wahine-o-Puna by Kirch) and Kanaloakua'ana, but his reign was 'either ineffectual or simply not recognized by the district chiefs'. Political fragmentation followed.[475] 5a. Keakamahana (mid-17th century CE).[476][477] Inherited the throne as daughter of Keakea-lani-kāne, but only controlled three leeward districts.[478] According to Valeri, Iwikauikaua 'served as [her] active coruler'.[479] Fornander says that Keakamahana and Iwikauikaua were husband and wife.[480] 5b. Iwikauikaua (mid-17th century CE).[481][482] According to Valeri, he served as active coruler with Keakamahana, who controlled three leeward districts on the Big Island.[483] Fornander says that Keakamahana and Iwikauikaua were husband and wife.[484] 6a. Keakea-lani-wahine (late 17th century CE).[485][486] Inherited the throne as daughter of Keakamahana, but ruled 'only the three leeward districts'.[487] Apparently ruled in a diarchy with Mahi'ololī, the 'general in chief'.[488] 6b. Mahi'ololī (late 17th century CE).[489][490] 'In his role as "kuhina kaua nui, 'general in chief,'” Mahi'ololī apparently served as Keakea-lani-wahine's active coruler'.[491] 7a. Keawe-i-kekahi-ali'i-o-ka-moku (shortened to Keawe, c. 1690-1700 CE).[492][493] Inherited the throne as son of Keakea-lani-wahine,[494] and seems to have ruled in a diarchy with his half-sister Kalani-kauleleaiwi.[495] Keawe used marriage alliances to unify the Big Island.[496] 7b. Kalani-kauleleaiwi (c. 1690-1700 CE).[497][498] Inherited the throne as daughter of Keakea-lani-wahine,[499] and seems to have ruled in a diarchy with her half-brother Keawe.[500] 8a. Kalanike'eaumoku (c. 1700-10 CE).[501][502] He was the son of Keawe and seems to have inherited control of Kona and Kohala while his half-brother Kalaninui'īamamao ruled Ka'ū.[503][504] 8b. Kalaninui'īamamao (c. 1700-10 CE).[505][506] He was the son of Keawe and seems to have inherited control of Ka'ū while his half-brother Kalanike'eaumoku ruled Kona and Kohala.[507][508] 9. Alapa'inui (c. 1710-54 CE).[509][510] 10. Keawe-'ōpala (c. 1754-55 CE).[511][512] Inherited the kingship as son of Alapa'inui,[513] but his 'reign was short, perhaps less than a year'.[514] 11. Kalani'ōpu'u (c. 1755-82 CE).[515][516] As the son of Kalaninui'īamamao (who was killed before Alapa'inui seized power), he inherited control of Ka'ū.[517][518] During Alapa'inui's reign he expanded his territory, then shortly after Keawe-'ōpala became ali'i nui, he defeated him in battle and usurped the throne. He was still in power by the time of Captain Cook's arrival in the archipelago in 1778 CE, and died in early 1782.[519]
♠ Number of contested transitions ♣ 8 ♥ 1.'Umi-a-Līloa (c. 1580-90 CE).[520][521] After ruling for a short time in what Hommon describes as a 'diarchy' with Hākau, 'Umi 'defeated Hākau and sacrificed him to Kū-kā'ili-moku (the war-god worshiped by the ali'i nui of Hawai'i Island), whereupon the chiefs of the five districts other than his own (Hāmākua) rose in rebellion. He conquered each district and then moved his court from Waipi'o valley, Hāmākua, to Kailua, Kona'.[522] 2. Keawenui a 'Umi (c. 1590-1610 CE).[523][524] 'He inherited direct control of Hilo, Puna, and Ka'ū districts from 'Umi ... Later, on the advice of his chiefs, he defeated his brother Keli'iokāloa ... to become ali'i nui.'[525] 3. Keakea-lani-kāne (mid-17th century CE).[526][527] After Lonoikamakahiki's death, he inherited the kingship as son of Kaikilaninui-ali'i-wahine-o-puna (called Kaikilani-Ali'i-Wahine-o-Puna by Kirch) and Kanaloakua'ana, but his reign was 'either ineffectual or simply not recognized by the district chiefs'. Political fragmentation followed.[528] 4a. Keakea-lani-wahine (late 17th century CE).[529][530] Inherited the throne as daughter of Keakamahana, but ruled 'only the three leeward districts'.[531] Apparently ruled in a diarchy with Mahi'ololī, the 'general in chief'.[532] Fornander reported that her reign was 'a troubled one': she faced opposition from 'the great house of I,' chiefs of Hilo district.[533] I have inferred that this transition would have been contested. 4b. Mahi'ololī (late 17th century CE).[534][535] 'In his role as "kuhina kaua nui, 'general in chief,'” Mahi'ololī apparently served as Keakea-lani-wahine's active coruler'.[536] Fornander reported that Keakea-lani-wahine's reign was 'a troubled one': she faced opposition from 'the great house of I,' chiefs of Hilo district.[537] I have inferred that this transition would have been contested. 5a. Kalanike'eaumoku (c. 1700-10 CE).[538][539] He was the son of Keawe and seems to have inherited control of Kona and Kohala while his half-brother Kalaninui'īamamao ruled Ka'ū.[540][541] His reign was contested: on the death of the previous ali'i nui, Keawe, a chief called Mokulani 'declared himself independent of Kalanikeeaumoku, who was apparently unable to enforce his claims as Moi of Hawaii'.[542] 5b. Kalaninui'īamamao (c. 1700-10 CE).[543][544] He was the son of Keawe and seems to have inherited control of Ka'ū while his half-brother Kalanike'eaumoku ruled Kona and Kohala.[545][546] I think we can infer that his reign was contested, like that of his half-brother and coruler - on the death of the previous ali'i nui, Keawe, a chief called Mokulani 'declared himself independent of Kalanikeeaumoku, who was apparently unable to enforce his claims as Moi of Hawaii'.[547] We also know that Kalanike'eaumoku and Kalaninui'īamamao quarrelled, and the latter was eventually killed (or 'caused to be killed') by the former.[548] 6. Alapa'inui (c. 1710-54 CE).[549][550] He came from a line of Kohala district chiefs known as the Mahi family, and with the help of some Kohala ali'i managed to defeat Kalanike'eaumoku and become ali'i nui of the entire Big Island.[551] His reign was certainly contested: the unification of the island and the defeat of the Maui king Kekaulike, who launched an invasion of Hawai'i, was achieved only through military strength,[552] and even after this he faced 'increasing opposition from internal parties'.[553] 7. Keawe-'ōpala (c. 1730-31 CE?).[554][555] Inherited the kingship as son of Alapa'inui,[556] but his 'reign was short, perhaps less than a year'.[557] Various chiefs rebelled against the new king after he deprived them of their lands.[558] 8. Kalani'ōpu'u (c. 1755-82 CE).[559][560] As the son of Kalaninui'īamamao (who was killed before Alapa'inui seized power), he inherited control of Ka'ū.[561][562] During Alapa'inui's reign he expanded his territory, then shortly after Keawe-'ōpala became ali'i nui, he defeated him in battle and usurped the throne. I think it is safe to assume that his accession would have been contested by the supporters of Alapa'inui and his chosen heir and son, Keawe-'ōpala. NB: I have assumed here that in the case of usurpation or a coup, the transition would have been contested.
♠ Number of successful overturns ♣ 4 ♥ 1.'Umi-a-Līloa (c. 1580-90 CE).[563][564] After ruling for a short time in what Hommon describes as a 'diarchy' with Hākau, 'Umi 'defeated Hākau and sacrificed him to Kū-kā'ili-moku (the war-god worshiped by the ali'i nui of Hawai'i Island), whereupon the chiefs of the five districts other than his own (Hāmākua) rose in rebellion. He conquered each district and then moved his court from Waipi'o valley, Hāmākua, to Kailua, Kona'.[565] 2. Keawenui a 'Umi (c. 1590-1610 CE).[566][567] 'He inherited direct control of Hilo, Puna, and Ka'ū districts from 'Umi ... Later, on the advice of his chiefs, he defeated his brother Keli'iokāloa ... to become ali'i nui.'[568] 3. Alapa'inui (c. 1710-54 CE).[569][570] He came from a line of Kohala district chiefs known as the Mahi family, and with the help of some Kohala ali'i managed to defeat Kalanike'eaumoku and become ali'i nui of the entire Big Island.[571] This represented an overturn of power by a rival chiefly line.[572] 4. Kalani'ōpu'u (c. 1755-82 CE).[573][574] As the son of Kalaninui'īamamao (who was killed before Alapa'inui seized power), he inherited control of Ka'ū.[575][576] During Alapa'inui's reign he expanded his territory, then shortly after Keawe-'ōpala became ali'i nui, he defeated him in battle and usurped the throne.

Central Bureaucracy[edit]

♠ State-level administrators ♣ present ♥ Kirch describes the political structure of the states of the Hawaiian archipelago by the time of European contact: 'At the apex of the polity sat the king, the ali'i nui or "great ali'i," ... The ali'i nui ruled over the entire mokupuni [a large island such as Hawai'i], assisted by various administrative aides'.[577] 'As was common in many dynastic states (Elias 1983), the central administration was effectively one and the same as the royal household, an incipient bureaucracy embedded within the king's extended residential group'.[578]

At various points during this polity, the Big Island was split into two or more polities, for example the 'leeward and windward confederacies into which [the island] was divided between about 1640 and 1740'.[579] However, these smaller polities still likely functioned as states, with a 'nascent' government and centralized bureaucracy.[580]


♠ Prestige of state-level bureaucracy ♣ equal ♥ 'As was common in many dynastic states ... the central administration was effectively one and the same as the royal household, an incipient bureaucracy embedded within the king's extended residential group'.[581] No real distinction can be drawn between 'top nobility' and 'top officials'.

Authority over state-level administrators

♠ Direct ♣ present ♥ The Hawaiian king's relationship to state-level administrators in this period included a mixture of direct and indirect control. 'When a king came to power, he had the right to redistribute governmental offices and land units to relatives and allies'.[582] He appointed both 'line and staff officers to manage the affairs of the kingdom', and the core of his court was populated by his personal chiefly advisors.[583] We can posit direct control by the chief executive at this highest level. However, the king also exercised indirect control in that he delegated responsibility throughout a branching administrative structure consisting of at least four strata of officials.[584]
♠ Indirect ♣ present ♥ The Hawaiian king's relationship to state-level administrators in this period included a mixture of direct and indirect control. 'When a king came to power, he had the right to redistribute governmental offices and land units to relatives and allies'.[585] He appointed both 'line and staff officers to manage the affairs of the kingdom', and the core of his court was populated by his personal chiefly advisors.[586] We can posit direct control by the chief executive at this highest level. However, the king also exercised indirect control in that he delegated responsibility throughout a branching administrative structure consisting of at least four strata of officials.[587]
♠ Diffused ♣ absent ♥ The Hawaiian king's relationship to state-level administrators in this period included a mixture of direct and indirect control. 'When a king came to power, he had the right to redistribute governmental offices and land units to relatives and allies'.[588] He appointed both 'line and staff officers to manage the affairs of the kingdom', and the core of his court was populated by his personal chiefly advisors.[589] We can posit direct control by the chief executive at this highest level. However, the king also exercised indirect control in that he delegated responsibility throughout a branching administrative structure consisting of at least four strata of officials.[590]
♠ Mixed ♣ present ♥ The Hawaiian king's relationship to state-level administrators in this period included a mixture of direct and indirect control. 'When a king came to power, he had the right to redistribute governmental offices and land units to relatives and allies'.[591] He appointed both 'line and staff officers to manage the affairs of the kingdom', and the core of his court was populated by his personal chiefly advisors.[592] We can posit direct control by the chief executive at this highest level. However, the king also exercised indirect control in that he delegated responsibility throughout a branching administrative structure consisting of at least four strata of officials.[593]
♠ Governmental control ♣ ♥
♠ Non-governmental oversight ♣ ♥

Source of Support of state-level officials

♠ State salary ♣ present ♥ It appears that the state-level officials surrounding the king were supported by the distribution of tax revenues collected during the Makahiki ceremonies: after making sacrifices to the gods, the king shared out the 'accumulated property' among the priests, important members of the court, and finally the lesser chiefs and 'military companies' throughout the land.[594] I have interpreted this as a state salary.
♠ Governed population ♣ ♥ It appears that the state-level officials surrounding the king were supported by the distribution of tax revenues collected during the Makahiki ceremonies: after making sacrifices to the gods, the king shared out the 'accumulated property' among the priests, important members of the court, and finally the lesser chiefs and 'military companies' throughout the land.[595] However, I have found nothing to suggest that state-level officials did not also draw revenue directly from the governed population, perhaps via konohiki land managers under their control.
♠ Land ♣ ♥
♠ None ♣ absent ♥ Kirch writes that from the beginning of this period, the ali'i nui and the other ali'i began to manage the 'highly productive' agricultural field systems 'more intensely, drawing on the surplus production to support a burgeoning elite hierarchy and incipient state administration'.[596]

♠ Abuse of power ♣ ♥

♠ Professionalization of state-level bureaucracy ♣ present ♥ The Hawaiian court incorporated 'specialists whose job it was to memorize genealogies, traditions, and important information of all kinds'.[597] Although there was no writing system, there were still professional bureaucrats in this 'highly elaborated oral-aural culture'.

Local-level officials (provincial, regional, civic administration)[edit]

♠ Number of regional administrative units ♣ 6: 1580 CE; 6: 1700 CE; 8: 1777 CE ♥ There were 6 moku divisions on the Big Island. Moku'āina or moku - often glossed as 'districts' - 'represent the territorial building blocks by which Hawaiian chiefdoms were organized and, at times, integrated'.[598] They encompassed multiple smaller ahupua'a, 'community territories'.[599] According to oral tradition, it was 'Umi, the first ali'i nui of this polity, who divided the Big Island into 'six great districts: Kau, Puna, Hilo, Hamakua, Kohala, and Kona'.[600] By the time of Cook's arrival, these moku boundaries were still in place: 'the names of the six moku divisions of Hawai'i Island were recorded on the very first map drawn by Captain Cook in 1778'.[601] Explanation for the changing number of regional units: under both 'Umi (r. 1580-90 CE) and Keawe (r. 1690-1700 CE) the entire Big Island was unified.[602][603] By the time of Cook's arrival, two districts in Maui had been conquered and added to this polity.[604]

♠ Local-level administrators ♣ present ♥ 'Each ahupua'a [territorial land unit] was controlled by a lesser chief, who in turn appointed one or more stewards to oversee production, organize work parties, collect tribute, and in other ways represent the chief'.[605] The term konohiki was applied to 'persons of intermediate rank who administered the divisions of the domain'.[606]

Authority over local-level administrators

♠ Direct ♣ absent ♥ The ali'i nui (king or high chief) had considerable power over the appointment and removal of lesser chiefs (ali'i-'ai-moku, district chiefs, and and ali'i-'ai-ahupua'a, chiefs of smaller, community-level territories).[607] He 'had the power to reallocate lands to the ali'i-'ai-moku and ali'i-'ai-ahupua'a under him'.[608] However, the konohiki land managers who administered the ahupua'a answered to the local chiefs rather than the king himself, so I have interpreted this as a form of indirect control over local-level administrators.
♠ Indirect ♣ present ♥ The ali'i nui (king or high chief) had considerable power over the appointment and removal of lesser chiefs (ali'i-'ai-moku, district chiefs, and and ali'i-'ai-ahupua'a, chiefs of smaller, community-level territories).[609] He 'had the power to reallocate lands to the ali'i-'ai-moku and ali'i-'ai-ahupua'a under him'.[610] However, the konohiki land managers who administered the ahupua'a answered to the local chiefs rather than the king himself, so I have interpreted this as a form of indirect control over local-level administrators.
♠ Diffused ♣ absent ♥ The ali'i nui (king or high chief) had considerable power over the appointment and removal of lesser chiefs (ali'i-'ai-moku, district chiefs, and and ali'i-'ai-ahupua'a, chiefs of smaller, community-level territories).[611] He 'had the power to reallocate lands to the ali'i-'ai-moku and ali'i-'ai-ahupua'a under him'.[612] However, the konohiki land managers who administered the ahupua'a answered to the local chiefs rather than the king himself, so I have interpreted this as a form of indirect control over local-level administrators.
♠ Mixed ♣ absent ♥ The ali'i nui (king or high chief) had considerable power over the appointment and removal of lesser chiefs (ali'i-'ai-moku, district chiefs, and and ali'i-'ai-ahupua'a, chiefs of smaller, community-level territories).[613] He 'had the power to reallocate lands to the ali'i-'ai-moku and ali'i-'ai-ahupua'a under him'.[614] However, the konohiki land managers who administered the ahupua'a answered to the local chiefs rather than the king himself, so I have interpreted this as a form of indirect control over local-level administrators.
♠ Governmental control ♣ ♥
♠ Non-governmental oversight ♣ ♥

Source of Support of local-level officials

♠ State salary ♣ present ♥ Konohiki land managers and local chiefs both received tribute from the maka'āinana (commoners).[615] These taxation rights seem to have been their source of support for much of the year, rather than a state salary. However, we know that during the four-month-long Makahiki ceremonies, which included tax collection, Hawaiian rulers could 'engender loyalty in government officials through the distribution of tax receipts'.[616] After tax revenues from the Makahiki had been distributed to the king's court, high priests and so on, a portion went to 'the chiefs ... throughout the land ... To the more important chiefs, with many followers, was given a large portion; to the lesser chiefs, with fewer followers, a smaller portion'.[617] I have interpreted this as a state salary.
♠ Governed population ♣ present ♥ Konohiki land managers and local chiefs both received tribute from the maka'āinana (commoners).[618] Sometimes they would also make 'arbitrary confiscations'.[619]
♠ Land ♣ ♥
♠ None ♣ absent ♥ Konohiki land managers and local chiefs both received tribute from the maka'āinana (commoners).[620] Sometimes they would also make 'arbitrary confiscations'.[621]

Legal System[edit]

♠ Multiple legal systems ♣ inferred absent ♥ The kapu system of regulations 'controlled the daily lives and bodily practices of all ranks of late Hawaiian society',[622] Kuykendall writes that 'there was a fairly large body of traditional or customary law relating mainly to such subjects as water rights, fishing rights, and land usage, and this customary law was ordinarily respected'.[623] However, kapu and customary land rights cannot be counted as independent legal systems with their own courts and judiciaries - there were no real courts of law in pre-contact Hawai'i.[624][625]

When we talk about the Hawaiian legal system in this period, we are essentially talking about the kapu system. Kirch describes this as 'a whole series of socially and ritually prescribed practices that controlled the daily lives and bodily practices of all ranks of late Hawaiian society',[626] and Seto Levin defines it as a 'system of legal proscriptions sanctioned by religious belief and enforced by the secular power of the political authority'.[627] The kapu regulations remained central to Hawaiian society until their 'overthrow' in 1819.[628]

Kuykendall writes: 'Generally speaking, the will of the ruling chief was the law of the land, but there was a fairly large body of traditional or customary law relating mainly to such subjects as water rights, fishing rights, and land usage, and this customary law was ordinarily respected'.[629] I have not considered the presence of customary law as justifying a 'present' code for multiple legal systems because I have not found descriptions of judicial process for community-level dispute resolution. If the practice known as ho'oponopono ('setting things right'), a form of dispute resolution under the guidance of respected community members, described in the 20th century, was also found during this period, we could code 'present' for non-state judiciary under 'informal justice'.[630] I have included a question to experts about this.

♠ Courts are supported by the state ♣ inferred absent ♥ There were no real courts of law where criminal trials were held in pre-contact Hawai'i. Pu'uhonua refuge sites served as 'quasi-judicial institution[s]', where transgressors could be pardoned,[631] but there was no weighing up of evidence for and against the defendant. All that was required was that they reach the pu'honua (sometimes a difficult task).[632] Outside the pu'uhonua, chiefs and kings had the power of life and death over the people. A chiefly court included a 'head executioner', the ilāmuku, who was 'responsible for putting to death anyone who violated the kapu of the noble'.[633] There is no mention in the sources of trials to determine guilt. Although the testimony of David Malo, a Native Hawaiian Christian convert born in the late 18th century, should not be taken at face value, it is telling that he wrote: 'There was no judge, nor any court of justice, to sit in judgment on wrong-doers of any sort'.[634]

When we talk about the Hawaiian legal system in this period, we are essentially talking about the kapu system. Kirch describes this as 'a whole series of socially and ritually prescribed practices that controlled the daily lives and bodily practices of all ranks of late Hawaiian society',[635] and Seto Levin defines it as a 'system of legal proscriptions sanctioned by religious belief and enforced by the secular power of the political authority'.[636] The kapu regulations remained central to Hawaiian society until their 'overthrow' in 1819.[637]

Kuykendall writes: 'Generally speaking, the will of the ruling chief was the law of the land, but there was a fairly large body of traditional or customary law relating mainly to such subjects as water rights, fishing rights, and land usage, and this customary law was ordinarily respected'.[638] I have not considered the presence of customary law as justifying a 'present' code for multiple legal systems because I have not found descriptions of judicial process for community-level dispute resolution. If the practice known as ho'oponopono ('setting things right'), a form of dispute resolution under the guidance of respected community members, described in the 20th century, was also found during this period, we could code 'present' for non-state judiciary under 'informal justice'.[639] I have included a question to experts about this.

♠ Criminal Prosecution ♣ inferred absent ♥ The pu'uhonua enclosure, a place of refuge for kapu-breakers and those fleeing war, has been described as a 'quasi-judicial institution with authority to grant refuge, absolution, and stays of execution, as well as amnesty'.[640] However, they do not seem to have been the settings for criminal prosecution in the true sense: as far as I can tell there was no trial in which evidence in favour of or against the defendant was weighed up. 'Ellis tells us that sanctuary and absolution were offered to "whomsoever" required it, regardless of the nature of the offense or the political allegiances of the offender'.[641] All that was required was that they reach the pu'honua (sometimes a difficult task).[642] Outside the pu'uhonua, chiefs and kings had the power of life and death over the people. A chiefly court included a 'head executioner', the ilāmuku, who was 'responsible for putting to death anyone who violated the kapu of the noble'.[643] There is no mention in the sources of trials to determine guilt. Although the testimony of David Malo, a Native Hawaiian Christian convert born in the late 18th century, should not be taken at face value, it is telling that he wrote: 'There was no judge, nor any court of justice, to sit in judgment on wrong-doers of any sort'.[644]

Accessibility of Courts

♠ Courts in polity capital ♣ inferred absent ♥ There were no real courts of law where criminal trials were held in pre-contact Hawai'i. All that was required was that they reach the pu'honua (sometimes a difficult task).[686] Outside the pu'uhonua, chiefs and kings had the power of life and death over the people. A chiefly court included a 'head executioner', the ilāmuku, who was 'responsible for putting to death anyone who violated the kapu of the noble'.[687] There is no mention in the sources of trials to determine guilt.
♠ Courts in regional capitals ♣ inferred absent ♥ There were no real courts of law where criminal trials were held in pre-contact Hawai'i. Pu'uhonua refuge sites served as 'quasi-judicial institution[s]', where transgressors could be pardoned,[688] but there was no weighing up of evidence for and against the defendant. All that was required was that they reach the pu'honua (sometimes a difficult task).[689] Outside the pu'uhonua, chiefs and kings had the power of life and death over the people. A chiefly court included a 'head executioner', the ilāmuku, who was 'responsible for putting to death anyone who violated the kapu of the noble'.[690] There is no mention in the sources of trials to determine guilt.
♠ Courts in provincial towns ♣ inferred absent ♥ There were no real courts of law where criminal trials were held in pre-contact Hawai'i. Pu'uhonua refuge sites served as 'quasi-judicial institution[s]', where transgressors could be pardoned,[691] but there was no weighing up of evidence for and against the defendant. All that was required was that they reach the pu'honua (sometimes a difficult task).[692] Outside the pu'uhonua, chiefs and kings had the power of life and death over the people. A chiefly court included a 'head executioner', the ilāmuku, who was 'responsible for putting to death anyone who violated the kapu of the noble'.[693] There is no mention in the sources of trials to determine guilt.
