Informal Justice[edit]

♠ Non-state judiciary ♣ ♥ If the practice known as ho'oponopono ('setting things right'), a form of dispute resolution under the guidance of respected community members, described in the 20th century, was also found during this period, we could code 'present' for non-state judiciary under 'informal justice'.[694] I have included a question to experts about this.

♠ Religious judiciary ♣ ♥

Property Rights[edit]

♠ Private property (land) ♣ absent ♥ In this period, all land in theory belonged to the ali'i nui (king), who 'distributed revocable land grants to lesser chiefs', who in turn gave temporary land grants to their followers.[695] 'The grants specified certain rights of use for the chiefs and allowed them to collect taxes from extended families of commoners who also retained restricted rights of occupancy and use'.[696] Marshall Sahlins and Patrick Kirch have both argued that as Hawaiian society increased in complexity from initial colonization to the moment of European contact in the late 18th century, older Polynesian landholding patterns - in which rights to land were held by lineage groups - were eroded by 'the aggrandizement of the chiefship. At European contact, land was divided into territorial units, each under the control of a chief; commoners, the maka'āinana, validated their rights to land by annual payment of tribute to chiefs'.[697] Valeri has compared this to 'European feudalism, in particular the seigneurie.'[698] The Board of Commissioners to Quiet Land Titles, appointed by Kamehameha III in 1846 as part of the Māhele land reforms, conducted a 'careful examination of the traditional system of land rights'.[699] They wrote (in a report published decades later) that: 'When the islands were conquered by Kamehameha I, he followed the example of his predecessors, and divided out the lands among his principal warrior chiefs, retaining however, a portion in his hands, to be cultivated or managed by his own immediate servants or attendants. Each principal chief divided his lands anew, and gave them out to an inferior order of chiefs, or persons of rank, by whom they were subdivided again and again, after passing through the hands of four, five or six persons, from the King down to the lowest class of tenants. All these persons were considered to have rights in the lands, or the productions of them. The proportions of these rights were not very clearly defined, but were nevertheless universally acknowledged'.[700] So here we have a complex system of land tenure, in which multiple chiefs at different layers in the hierarchy had nested claims to 'ownership' of any given piece of land, and commoners were considered to be tenants with labour and tribute obligations to their 'landlords'.[701] Ali'i or konohiki (land managers) at various levels could revoke their sub-tenants' rights to the land and redistribute plots among their followers.[702]

♠ Usufruct ♣ present ♥ Commoners had conditional rights to farm their assigned plots and enjoy the fruits of their labour.[703] In the case of kama'āina ('children of the land'), '[f]amilies that had been resident on their ... plots for two or more generations',[704] these rights seem to have been relatively secure. Nevertheless, in order to retain their usufruct rights, all maka'āinana had to render tribute and labour to the hierarchy of chiefs above them.[705]
♠ Exclusivity ♣ inferred present ♥ We know from 19th-century records that the typical ahupua'a, the division of land below the moku (district) level, 'tended to be economically self-sufficient ... Nineteenth-century land records indicate that a corollary of ahupua'a self-sufficiency was that commoners of other communities were allowed use of resources only with the residents' permission'.[706] Here we see that ahupua'a residents have exclusivity rights at the community-wide level. Perhaps this principle also worked at the level of individual plots?
♠ Partibility ♣ present ♥ Each chief who had received a (revocable) land grant from a chief above him in the hierarchy would divide the land, giving revocable land grants to those below him and maintaining rights to a portion of the agricultural produce.[707] The Board of Commissioners to Quiet Land Titles, appointed by Kamehameha III in 1846 as part of the Māhele land reforms, conducted a 'careful examination of the traditional system of land rights'.[708] They wrote (in a report published decades later) that: 'When the islands were conquered by Kamehameha I, he followed the example of his predecessors, and divided out the lands among his principal warrior chiefs, retaining however, a portion in his hands, to be cultivated or managed by his own immediate servants or attendants. Each principal chief divided his lands anew, and gave them out to an inferior order of chiefs, or persons of rank, by whom they were subdivided again and again, after passing through the hands of four, five or six persons, from the King down to the lowest class of tenants. All these persons were considered to have rights in the lands, or the productions of them. The proportions of these rights were not very clearly defined, but were nevertheless universally acknowledged'.[709]
♠ Lease ♣ present ♥ In one sense, all commoners (and lesser chiefs) were 'tenants' of the land, owing tribute to their higher-status chiefly landlords.[710] Hommon notes that the tribute paid by commoners can be seen either as taxes or 'rent due the chiefs in their role as landlords'.[711]
♠ Lease Rights ♣ inferred absent ♥ I have not come across any mention of legal protections for tenant commoners working the land: chiefs and konohiki land managers could confiscate and redistribute the land as they saw fit,[712] and missionary observers after contact also noted that chiefs made 'arbitrary confiscations' of the commoners' agricultural produce.[713] Rather than appealing to legally enshrined protections, commoners had recourse to several different strategies when a chief or konohiki became oppressive. Because they were not bound to the soil (enserfed), they could 'vote with their feet' and move elsewhere. 'Some families worked plots of land under different chiefs (or on different islands) as insurance against both arbitrary behavior by their landlord and local famine or drought'.[714] Alternatively, they could (and often did) mount a rebellion.[715]
♠ Transfer of property ♣ ♥
♠ Use of property ♣ present ♥ Within an ahupua'a (self-contained economic territory, smaller than a moku district), '[a] significant number of fields were designated as kō'ele plots, under the direct supervision of the land manager or konohiki. The productions of these plots were used for the support of the konohiki, his chief, and further on up the hierarchy ... the labor required to cultivate these plots had to be supplied by the maka'āinana.[716] Here we see that when an ali'i makes a land grant to his inferior, it is with the proviso that certain portions of the land be set aside as kō'ele plots.
♠ Property market ♣ absent ♥ The traditional Hawaiian system of land tenure was based on revocable land grants from superior chiefs to inferior chiefs (and ultimately to commoners, the lowest level of tenant).[717] Chiefs at any level in the hierarchy had the power to reallocate portions of their lands to different people, but this was not a sale of land, rather a transfer of certain restricted and revocable rights to the land and its agricultural produce. In addition, 'there was no money (in Diamond's words, no "abstract, intrinsically valueless medium for appropriating surplus, storing value, and deferring payment or delaying exchange") in precontact Hawai'i'.[718]

♠ Other property ownership ♣ ♥

Inheritance System

♠ partible inheritance ♣ ♥
♠ patrilineal inheritance ♣ present ♥ Writing about traditional Hawaiian social organization before the Māhele land reforms, Linnekin asserts that although 'Kinship was reckoned bilaterally ... The evidence points to a patrilineal bias in political and property succession'.[719]
♠ matrilineal inheritance ♣ ♥
♠ primogeniture inheritance by only males ♣ ♥
♠ primogeniture inheritance by males and females ♣ ♥ Hommon tells us that 'among both commoners and chiefs of Hawai'i and Polynesia generally, the separate term for elder sibling reflects the principle of primogeniture, the superior status of the firstborn'.[720] Likewise, Goldman discusses the importance of primogeniture among Hawaiian commoners and chiefs: 'All first-born, male or female, were ritually honored; and even though men were preferred as actual chiefs and as family heads, the rule of primogeniture finally outweighed the sex consideration'. In commoner families, the first-born was designated mua, a term referring to the 'forward end of the canoe'.[721] However, these authors are discussing the superior rank of first-born children rather than patterns of wealth inheritance.
♠ other inheritance practices ♣ present ♥ Linnekin writes that there were hierarchical landlord/tenant relationships even within individual families: 'A senior member of the household group was considered the haku 'āina "landlord" within the family and had the right to allocate use and residence rights to junior relatives and dependents who then lived "under" the donor'.[722]
♠ Trusts ♣ ♥
♠ State expropriation ♣ present ♥ Under the traditional Hawaiian system of land tenure, 'in theory all of the lands belonged to the king, for he alone had the right to allocate them to subservient chiefs, or to dispossess chiefs of land they had formerly held'.[723] David Malo wrote that 'It was the ma-ka-aina-na who did all the work on the land, yet all they produced from the soil belonged to the chiefs; and the power to expel a man from the land and rob him of his possessions lay with the chief'.[724]
♠ Non-state expropriation ♣ ♥

Equity[edit]

Sex Class and Race[edit]

Marriage

♠ Monogamy ♣ absent ♥ 'Marriage was a flexible arrangement in traditional Hawaiian culture. A woman could have more than one husband and a man more than one wife'.[725] Likewise, Ralston writes that 'Marital unions were important at all levels of society, but monogamy was not jealously demanded and life-long partnerships were not expected'.[726] Diamond gives the impression that marriage was generally an informal and fluid affair: 'While betrothals occurred, occasionally arranged by parents of chiefs or by other prominent persons, such formalized relationships were uncommon'.[727]
♠ Formal Elite Polygamy ♣ present ♥ Elites of both sexes could have multiple officially recognized 'spouses'. 'The primary wives of the elites, and especially the royal wives of the king, were carefully chosen from among the ali'i class. Royal marriage was highly prescribed'.[728]
♠ Informal Elite Polygamy ♣ absent ♥ The multiple spouses of the chiefly elite were formally recognized.[729]
♠ Elite Concubinage ♣ present ♥ Kirch says in his discussion of 'Late Hawaiian society' that 'In principle, if not wholly in practice, the classes were endogamous; the major exceptions consisted of the appropriation of exceptionally beautiful young women of the commoner class by elite males, but only as secondary wives or concubines'.[730] The distinction between wives and concubines may have been more blurry in 16th- to 18th-century Hawaii than in European contexts: 'marriage as is understood in the Western sense was not a feature of pre-contact Hawaiian culture'.[731] However, it seems that we can make a distinction between 'principal' and 'secondary' elite marriage. Kirch says that 'officially sanctioned unions were class endogamous',[732] and Linnekin distinguishes between 'principal' chiefly marriages between two highly ranked partners, often close blood-relatives, and the 'secondary' unions of male chiefs with commoners. The latter were desirable because they could surround the chiefs/kings with lower ranked but loyal retainers: 'The chief's child by a commoner should be the perfect retainer, destined to serve by virtue of low birth and enjoined to loyalty by close kinship. Through hypogamous unions chiefs attempt to constitute their retinue as a kindred'.[733] I think we can consider the commoner women who became the lovers of ali'i (and bore them children) as meeting the criteria for 'concubines'.
♠ Typical number of extramarital partners ♣ few ♥ Malo claimed that traditionally, 'It was a common thing for a chief to have children by this and that woman with whom he had enjoyed secret amours. Some of these children were recognized and some were not recognized'.[734] I have coded 'few' because there is no indication that the number of elite extramarital unions that produced children routinely exceeded ten.

Legal rights are different on the basis of:

♠ Gender ♣ present ♥ Within the kapu system of 'legal proscriptions sanctioned by religious belief and enforced by the secular power of the political authority',[735] the 'ai kapu '"eating taboo" ... specified that men and women could not eat together ... and that certain foods ... were prohibited to women'.[736] In general, women seem to have been subject to more restrictions on their diet, and '[t]he penalty for infraction in any of these areas was death'.[737] Although 'it can be argued that the differentiation of men and women indexed by the tabus was less salient than the rank distinction between chiefs and commoners',[738] we can still see that 'legal rights' (and penalties for transgression) were different for men and women.
♠ Social class ♣ present ♥ The elaborate Hawaiian kapu system encompassed a 'set of prohibitions and strictures regulating contact between the ali'i (kapu) and the commoners (noa)'.[739] Different social classes were subject to different kapu restrictions, and harsh penalties applied in the case of transgression. For instance, David Malo recorded that 'when a tabu chief ate, the people in his presence must kneel, and if anyone raised his knee from the ground, he was put to death'.[740]
♠ Ethnicity or race ♣ inferred absent ♥ Before European contact, there were no real ethnic/racial groups marked as different from the rest of the population. Kirch and Green note the appearance in the Proto-Polynesian language of the term *tangata ma(a)qoli, meaning 'indigenous person, one of our own kind'.[741] This became maoli in Hawaiian. However, because 'the tangata ma(a)qoli were invariably the first human occupants on island after island as these were discovered and settled, it was unnecessary to have a contrastive term, hence the confusing diversity of appellations which were given to the European "invaders" when these appeared from the seventeenth century onwards'.[742]

Discrimination[edit]

Discrimination on the basis of gender (include de facto discrimination even when there are no explicit legal restrictions)

♠ Bureaucratic positions are open to both males and females ♣ inferred present ♥ The chiefly class, ali'i, often acted in the role of state officials, principally concerned with land management.[743] Both men and women (for instance, the high-ranking chiefess Ka'ahumanu, active in the later polity USHawai) seem to have taken on these duties.[744][745] 'With their own households and retainers, female chiefs ... seem to have retained a separate but equal domain of authority'.[746]
♠ Promotion in administrative positions is open to both males and females ♣ ♥
♠ Education is available to both males and females ♣ present ♥ Hula dance performances, 'witnessed and enjoyed by people of all ranks' and performed by both men and women, were occasions for the recitation of Hawaii's extensive oral literature, which 'consisted of meles (songs) of various kinds, genealogies and honorific chants, stories and traditional lore in which were imbedded fragments of history and biography'.[747] Becoming a hula dancer required lengthy and formal training 'under a kumu-hula (teacher of the hula)',[748] and women may have predominated among hula practitioners in the late precontact era.[749]
♠ Property can be owned by both males and females ♣ present ♥ 'Land ownership' was a complex issue in Hawaiian society at this time, but various ali'i at multiple levels were considered to have rights in the land. Protestant missionary John Emerson, resident in the Anahulu Valley of O'ahu Island in the early 19th century, noted that 'every land has been regarded as having some owner, and many lands have six or eight owners at the same time'[750] - a description that would also apply to land tenure on the Big Island.[751] Female as well as male chiefs took on this role. For instance, in a series of letters to a konohiki land manager in the 1830s, Paulo Kānoa, the 'secretary' of the O'ahu chiefess Ka'ahumanu, 'made incessant demands' for agricultural produce, animals and goods.[752] Ka'ahumanu's rights to the productions of the ahupua'a community stemmed from her claims on its land.

Discrimination on the basis of social class (include de facto limitations even when there are no explicit legal restrictions)

♠ Bureaucratic positions are open to people from all social classes ♣ present ♥ Hommon writes: 'As kings, priests, war leaders, landlords, and bureaucrats, chiefs occupied virtually every administrative position of political, military, economic, and ritual power in ancient Hawai'i'.[753] However, at the lowest-status end of the spectrum, commoners could attain administrative positions through their connections with chiefs: 'An ahupua'a chief often added a non-ali'i [chiefly] stratum to the bureaucracy by recruiting his relatives or "big men," prominent common residents of 'ili, to serve as konohiki (stewards) who could help ensure that the community met its quotas'.[754]
♠ Promotion in administrative positions is open to people from all social classes ♣ inferred absent: 1580-1700CE; {absent; present}: 1700-1777 CE ♥ Hommon writes: 'As kings, priests, war leaders, landlords, and bureaucrats, chiefs occupied virtually every administrative position of political, military, economic, and ritual power in ancient Hawai'i'.[755] However, Goldman states that 'In the late period just before European contact' the office of kalaimoku, the island administrator, 'had begun to go to commoners'.[756]
♠ Education is available to people from all social classes ♣ present ♥ Hula dance performances, 'witnessed and enjoyed by people of all ranks' and performed by both men and women, were occasions for the recitation of Hawaii's extensive oral literature, which 'consisted of meles (songs) of various kinds, genealogies and honorific chants, stories and traditional lore in which were imbedded fragments of history and biography'.[757] N. B. Emerson, historian of the hula and son of a Protestant missionary to Hawai'i, wrote that for ancient Hawaiians, the hula took the 'place of our [Westerners'] concert-hall and lecture-room, our opera and theater'.[758]
♠ Property can be owned by people from all social classes ♣ present ♥ 'Land ownership' was a complex issue in Hawaii of this time, but all levels of the hierarchy from the commoners working the land to the ali'i nui at the top were considered to have some form of rights to the land. The Board of Commissioners to Quiet Land Titles, appointed by Kamehameha III in 1846 as part of the Māhele land reforms, conducted a 'careful examination of the traditional system of land rights'.[759] They wrote (in a report published decades later) that: 'When the islands were conquered by Kamehameha I, he followed the example of his predecessors, and divided out the lands among his principal warrior chiefs, retaining however, a portion in his hands, to be cultivated or managed by his own immediate servants or attendants. Each principal chief divided his lands anew, and gave them out to an inferior order of chiefs, or persons of rank, by whom they were subdivided again and again, after passing through the hands of four, five or six persons, from the King down to the lowest class of tenants. All these persons were considered to have rights in the lands, or the productions of them. The proportions of these rights were not very clearly defined, but were nevertheless universally acknowledged'.[760]

Discrimination on the basis of ethnicity/race

♠ Bureaucratic positions are open to people from all ethnic/racial groups ♣ ♥ Before European contact, there were no real ethnic/racial groups marked as different from the rest of the population. Kirch and Green note the appearance in the Proto-Polynesian language of the term *tangata ma(a)qoli, meaning 'indigenous person, one of our own kind'.[761] This became maoli in Hawaiian. However, because 'the tangata ma(a)qoli were invariably the first human occupants on island after island as these were discovered and settled, it was unnecessary to have a contrastive term, hence the confusing diversity of appellations which were given to the European "invaders" when these appeared from the seventeenth century onwards'.[762]
♠ Promotion in administrative positions is open to people from all ethnic/racial groups ♣ ♥ Before European contact, there were no real ethnic/racial groups marked as different from the rest of the population. Kirch and Green note the appearance in the Proto-Polynesian language of the term *tangata ma(a)qoli, meaning 'indigenous person, one of our own kind'.[763] This became maoli in Hawaiian. However, because 'the tangata ma(a)qoli were invariably the first human occupants on island after island as these were discovered and settled, it was unnecessary to have a contrastive term, hence the confusing diversity of appellations which were given to the European "invaders" when these appeared from the seventeenth century onwards'.[764]
♠ Education is available to people from all ethnic/racial groups ♣ ♥ Before European contact, there were no real ethnic/racial groups marked as different from the rest of the population. Kirch and Green note the appearance in the Proto-Polynesian language of the term *tangata ma(a)qoli, meaning 'indigenous person, one of our own kind'.[765] This became maoli in Hawaiian. However, because 'the tangata ma(a)qoli were invariably the first human occupants on island after island as these were discovered and settled, it was unnecessary to have a contrastive term, hence the confusing diversity of appellations which were given to the European "invaders" when these appeared from the seventeenth century onwards'.[766]
♠ Property can be owned by people from all ethnic/racial groups ♣ ♥ Before European contact, there were no real ethnic/racial groups marked as different from the rest of the population. Kirch and Green note the appearance in the Proto-Polynesian language of the term *tangata ma(a)qoli, meaning 'indigenous person, one of our own kind'.[767] This became maoli in Hawaiian. However, because 'the tangata ma(a)qoli were invariably the first human occupants on island after island as these were discovered and settled, it was unnecessary to have a contrastive term, hence the confusing diversity of appellations which were given to the European "invaders" when these appeared from the seventeenth century onwards'.[768]

Social Mobility[edit]

Status[edit]

Elite status

♠ elite status is hereditary ♣ present ♥ Kirch refers to the 'hereditary ali'i (elites) of Hawai'i', who by the time of Captain Cook's arrival at the end of the Hawaii 3 'had become a separate, endogamous class'.[769]
♠ elite status is selected ♣ present ♥ Goldman wrote that 'each new Hawaiian administration systematically expropriated its opponents, rewarded its supporters, and curtailed the influence of potential rivals by giving them estates smaller than due their rank. By this "spoils system" the Hawaiian rulers frankly acknowledged the equation: land-wealth equals power. The spoils system promoted social mobility among the upper ranks and left the lower ranks least disturbed'.[770] Here we see a system in which loyalty to the eventually victorious ruler could be translated into higher status.
♠ formal system of hereditary social status ♣ present ♥ Kirch writes that 'instead of sitting at the apex of a "conical clan," which ramified downward to incorporate the entire society, the hereditary ali'i (elites) of Hawai'i had become a separate, endogamous class'. The highest ali'i claimed descent from the gods; indeed, they claimed to be ali'i akua, "god-kings."'[771] This system of stratification was less fluid - more formalized - than in other Polynesian contexts or in earlier Hawaiian history.

♠ social mobility ♣ present ♥ Goldman wrote that 'each new Hawaiian administration systematically expropriated its opponents, rewarded its supporters, and curtailed the influence of potential rivals by giving them estates smaller than due their rank. By this "spoils system" the Hawaiian rulers frankly acknowledged the equation: land-wealth equals power. The spoils system promoted social mobility among the upper ranks and left the lower ranks least disturbed'.[772]

♠ recruitment of chief executive ♣ lower ♥ 'Umi-a-Līloa, the first ali'i nui of this polity, was the son of Līloa by a woman called Akahiakuleana, who was 'not regarded as being of true ali'i rank'.[773] As Kirch illustrates, the child of a high chief and a commoner woman was designated ali'i noanoa, meaning 'without kapu', and was 'not recognized as ali'i unless special provision [was] made'.[774] According to oral tradition, before he died, Līloa bestowed the kingdom on his higher-ranked heir Hākau but also, significantly, decreed that Kūkā'ilimoku, a manifestation of the god Kū in wickerwork and feathers, should go to 'Umi. 'Umi was eventually able to overthrow Hākau with an army raised up in the hinterlands of the Big Island.[775]
♠ extreme status elevation ♣ inferred absent ♥ I have not come across any mention of instances of extreme status elevation in Hawai'i during this period. ('Umi-a-Līloa, the first ali'i nui of this polity, had a mother who was not of 'true ali'i rank', but his father was the ali'i nui, so I have not counted him.[776]) Goldman writes in his account of 'status rivalry' in Polynesia that the Hawaiian 'spoils system', in which victorious rulers redistributed land their followers, 'promoted social mobility among the upper ranks and left the lower ranks least disturbed'.[777] Likewise, bureaucratic positions do not seem to have functioned as a route to higher status for commoners: 'As kings, priests, war leaders, landlords, and bureaucrats, chiefs occupied virtually every administrative position of political, military, economic, and ritual power in ancient Hawai'i'.[778]
♠ extreme status diminution ♣ present ♥ Anyone who broke a kapu prohibition became la'a, meaning 'cursed, defiled' or dangerously sacred,[779] and therefore a suitable victim for human sacrifice.[780] The sacrifice was not straightforward capital punishment, but was preceded by the kapu transgressor's descent into a state of defilement. Valeri writes that 'victims of human sacrifices are usually transgressors or rebels. By sacrificing them, the king purifies society from the pollution brought on by their sins and the disorders they have provoked'.[781]

♠ occupational mobility ♣ {absent; present} ♥ Lass presents a view of Hawaiian craft specialization in which certain families are associated with certain crafts, passing the necessary skills down over generations. This fits into a wider Polynesian pattern. 'Hawaiian crafting had a strong hereditary component with crafts being learned within the family and usually from elders. For example, in an account written in the early nineteenth century, an unidentified Native Hawaiian relates knowledge of canoe making that he apparently learned from his grandfather who was a canoe maker ... In Polynesia in general craft participation tended to be hereditary, and particular crafts were associated with particular families or descent groups'.[782] However, in his discussion of the priesthood, Goldman writes that although 'In the upper ranks, the kahuna title was hereditary', commoners could become kahuna after an 'apprenticeship'.[783]

Slavery[edit]

♠ Proportion of population enslaved ♣ unknown ♥ 'The numbers and role of kauwā in late Hawaiian society remain matters of considerable ambiguity. Kepelino ... implies that they were not numerous'.[784]

In late precontact and early contact-era Hawai'i: 'The papa kauwā, at the bottom of the social scale, are sometimes translated in Western literature as "slaves," but a better term is probably "outcast." Malo ... explains that kauwā was a general term of degradation that was widely used in a metaphorical sense, such as a younger brother referring to himself as the kauwā of his older (higher ranked) brother, or a retainer calling himself the kauwā of his chief. True kauwā, however, were a special category of persons (often physically marked by special tattoos on their faces) who could be taken for the purpose of human sacrifice at war temples'.[785]

♠ Proportion of population enserfed ♣ 0 ♥ The maka'āinana (commoners) were not enserfed: they were free to leave their agricultural plots and escape the authority of local ali'i if they became oppressive.[786] The kauwā class of 'outcasts', on the other hand, were described by Malo as bound to certain chiefs 'to the latest generation'.[787] Nevertheless, I have not coded them as enserfed here because as far as I can tell, they were not peasants working the land to produce food for chiefs. According to Malo they 'were said to be people from the wild woods (nahelehele), from the lowest depths',[788] and as a ritually charged, dangerous social group (they were 'as impure as the ali'i were pure'),[789] it seems very unlikely that chiefs could eat the fruits of their labour without breaking kapu.

In late precontact and early contact-era Hawai'i: 'The papa kauwā, at the bottom of the social scale, are sometimes translated in Western literature as "slaves," but a better term is probably "outcast." Malo ... explains that kauwā was a general term of degradation that was widely used in a metaphorical sense, such as a younger brother referring to himself as the kauwā of his older (higher ranked) brother, or a retainer calling himself the kauwā of his chief. True kauwā, however, were a special category of persons (often physically marked by special tattoos on their faces) who could be taken for the purpose of human sacrifice at war temples'.[790]

Types

♠ Chattel slavery ♣ inferred absent ♥ In late precontact and early contact-era Hawai'i: 'The papa kauwā, at the bottom of the social scale, are sometimes translated in Western literature as "slaves," but a better term is probably "outcast." Malo ... explains that kauwā was a general term of degradation that was widely used in a metaphorical sense, such as a younger brother referring to himself as the kauwā of his older (higher ranked) brother, or a retainer calling himself the kauwā of his chief. True kauwā, however, were a special category of persons (often physically marked by special tattoos on their faces) who could be taken for the purpose of human sacrifice at war temples'.[791] There is surprisingly little discussion of kauwā in the literature, but a passage in Malo's Hawaiian Antiquities suggests that we cannot view this as chattel slavery: 'Those who were kauwa to their chiefs and kings in the old times continued to be kauwa, and their descendants after them to the latest generations; also the descendants of the kings and chiefs, their masters, retained to the latest generation their position as masters'.[792] In Malo's description, kauwā could not be bought and sold like chattel slaves, but remained bound to a particular chiefly line.
♠ Elite slavery ♣ inferred absent ♥ A chief's retinue contained many servants, retainers and officials, but none of these appear to have been enslaved. David Malo wrote: 'Those who had charge of the chief's goods or who looked after his food were called kauwa. Their real name was 'a-'i-pu'u-pu'u and they were also called kauwa; but they were kauwa only in name, they were not really slaves ... The marshals or constables (ilamuku) of the king were spoken of as his kauwa; but they were not really kauwa. There were then many classes of people called or spoken of as kauwa, but they were kauwa only in name, to indicate their inferior rank; they were not really and in fact kauwa.'[793]
♠ Bonded serfdom ♣ absent ♥ The relationship between the commoners, the maka'āinana, and the ali'i elites in this period was effectively that of tenant to landlord.[794] Commoners' 'right to work the land was contingent on the regular payment of tribute (ho'okupu) [and labour] to the hierarchy of overlords'.[795] Hawaii diverged from the common Polynesian pattern, in which membership of a lineage conferred rights to the land for ordinary people:[796] land could be redistributed by elites at any time, although this tended to happen only after major transitions of power.[797] However, the maka'āinana were not 'serfs' because they were not bound to the land. La Croix and Roumasset write that 'the commoners in Hawaii, unlike the serfs in Europe, were not bound to the land or to specific ali'i. They were free to migrate to other chiefdoms in the Islands. In fact, some commoner families worked scattered plots of land under different chiefs as insurance against arbitrary or capricious behavior on the part of any one ruler'.[798]
♠ Corvée labor to a landowner ♣ present ♥ Commoners' rights to cultivate plots of land were contingent on their provision of goods and labour to the hierarchy of chiefs above them, who were considered to be the 'owners' of the land (with the king as ultimate owner).[799] 'A significant number of fields were designated as kō'ele plots, under the direct supervision of the land manager or konohiki ... The productions of the kō'ele plots were not only reserved for the konohiki and chiefs, but the labor required to cultivate these plots had to be supplied by the maka'āinana [commoners]'.[800]
♠ Corvée labor to the state ♣ present ♥ The Board of Commissioners to Quiet Land Titles, appointed by Kamehameha III in 1846 as part of the Māhele land reforms, conducted a 'careful examination of the traditional system of land rights'.[801] They wrote (in a report published decades later) that 'All persons possessing landed property, whether superior landlords, tenants or sub-tenants, owed and paid to the King not only a land tax, which he assessed at pleasure, but also, service which was called for at discretion, on all the grades from the highest down'.[802]

Restraints

♠ Owners can execute slaves ♣ inferred present ♥ People could be put to death by representatives of the ali'i ruling class for transgressions of kapu. For instance, 'the two highest ranks of ali'i, the nī'aupi'o and pi'o, were due a particular form of deference known as the kapu moe, the "prostrating taboo." Anyone failing to perform the kapu moe was subject to immediate death'.[803] The chiefs' power of life and death over the commoners seems likely also to have extended to those kauwā 'slaves' who were bound to certain chiefly lines.[804]
♠ Owners can impose corporeal punishment on slaves ♣ ♥ I have not been able to find any reference to corporal punishment of kauwā 'slaves' by the chiefs to whom they were bound.
♠ Corporeal punishment by the state ♣ present ♥ People could be put to death by representatives of the ali'i ruling class for transgressions of kapu. For instance, 'the two highest ranks of ali'i, the nī'aupi'o and pi'o, were due a particular form of deference known as the kapu moe, the "prostrating taboo." Anyone failing to perform the kapu moe was subject to immediate death'.[805] The entourage of a highly ranked ali'i would include a 'head executioner', who 'was responsible for putting to death anyone who violated the kapu of a noble'.[806]

Human sacrifice

♠ Prisoners ♣ present ♥ According to Bellah, war captives could fall into the kauwā class, 'from whom human sacrifices were taken'.[807] The rituals of Kū, performed by the king and his chief priest, required a human sacrifice. During times of war, this could take the form of 'the body of a vanquished chief or king' (who was presumably taken prisoner after being defeated).[808]
♠ Low social class ♣ present ♥ In late precontact Hawaiian society, members of the lowest class, the kauwā or 'outcasts', could be sacrificed at war temples.[809]
♠ Elites ♣ present ♥ The rituals of Kū, performed by the king and his chief priest, required a human sacrifice. During times of war, this could take the form of 'the body of a vanquished chief or king'.[810]
♠ Retainers ♣ [absent; present] ♥ Accounts from the succeeding period, USKameh, of the defeat and sacrifice of Kamehameha's rival, the Ka'ū chief Keōua, show that chiefly retainers were sometimes also sacrificed: 'When Keōua surrendered himself to Kamehameha, with certain knowledge that he would be killed and sacrificed to the god, many of his chiefs and personal retainers accompanied him to become his death companions (moepu'u)'.[811] I have not been able to discover how old this practice is in Hawai'i, but have included a question for experts.
♠ Outsiders ♣ inferred present ♥ The rituals of Kū, performed by the king and his chief priest, required a human sacrifice. During times of war, this could take the form of 'the body of a vanquished chief or king'.[812] Sacrificed rival kings must have come from outside the Big Island polity. Lieutenant King, a member of Cook's third voyage, wrote that the Big Island Hawaiians 'gave us to understand' that the skulls mounted on railings at the temple platform of Hikiau were 'those of Mowee [Maui] men, whom they had killed on the death of some Chief'.[813] Human sacrifice was being practised in Hawai'i by the time of the ali'i nui 'Umi (at the beginning of this polity), and was still a central part of state ritual in the late 18th century.[814]

Religion and Normative Ideology[edit]

♠ RA ♣ Enrico Cioni ♥

♠ Expert ♣ ♥

♠ Name of Official Cult ♣ Hawaiian Religion ♥

♠ alternate Names of Official Cult ♣ ♥

♠ Name of other significant religious or ideological systems ♣ ♥

♠ Description of the Normative Ideology ♣ ♥

Deification of Rulers[edit]

♠ Rulers are legitimated by gods ♣ inferred present ♥ "Polynesians located the ultimate source of power and fecundity in the spirit realm, abode of their ancestors and their gods; mana was the manifestation of that power in the world of humans. As Handy 1927:26) wrote: 'Mana was exhibited in persons, in power, strength, prestige, reputation, skill, dynamic personality, intelligence; in things, in efficacy, in ‘luck’; that is[,] in accomplishment.' Life literally depended on the continual transmission of mana from the gods to humans and earth, for mana was 'always linked to organic generativity and thus to all forces of growth and vitality' (Shore 1989:164).//But mana did not flow equally to all persons; quite the contrary, mana followed the pathways of rank and genealogical descent that also ordered Polynesian societies, and the more hierarchical these became, the more differentiated the distribution of mana across the social order. In all Polynesian societies, mana was concentrated in persons of rank, but in the more highly stratified societies the chiefs came to occupy particular roles as sources or vehicles of mana on which the society at large depended for its well-being. Nowhere was this more so than in late Hawaiian society. The Hawaiian divine kings, as gods on earth, were essential for the reproduction of society; they also held the power of life or death over the common people, most often exercised through the rites of human sacrifice. Death, as the polar opposite of life, is another aspect of mana." [815] NB This descriptive text refers to the period following European contact. The fact that certain elements of the ritual-religious system the Europeans encountered in Hawaii had probably been in place for a few centuries at first seemed sufficient to infer the presence of other elements, such as this one. However, this approach now seems too speculative.

♠ Rulers control supernatural energy ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Mana. "Polynesians located the ultimate source of power and fecundity in the spirit realm, abode of their ancestors and their gods; mana was the manifestation of that power in the world of humans. As Handy 1927:26) wrote: 'Mana was exhibited in persons, in power, strength, prestige, reputation, skill, dynamic personality, intelligence; in things, in efficacy, in ‘luck’; that is[,] in accomplishment.' ♠ Rulers are descendants of gods ♣ suspected unknown ♥ "The people—retaining the word makaainana as an etymological echo of the past, but now divorced from its former associations to lineage and land—had no genealogies extending farther back than grandparents, indeed were prevented from keeping them. In stark contrast the chiefs elaborated their ancestral connections back to the gods, and retained specialists to recite their name chants." [817] NB This descriptive text refers to the period following European contact. The fact that certain elements of the ritual-religious system the Europeans encountered in Hawaii had probably been in place for a few centuries at first seemed sufficient to infer the presence of other elements, such as this one. However, this approach now seems too speculative.

♠ Rulers become gods after death ♣ suspected unknown ♥ "The kuni priest took a part of the flesh of the dead king's body to be used as maunu in their incantations against those who had prayed him to death. The body was then taken to the mua house in the presence of the multitude and laid in the heiau, that it might be deified and transformed into an aumakua. [...] After this was accomplished, the bones were put in position and arranged to resemble the shape of a man, being seated in the house until the day of prayer, when their deification would take place and they would be addressed in prayer by the kahuna of the mua. The period of defilement was then at an end; consequently the king's successor was permitted to return, and the apotheosis of the dead king being accomplished, he was worshipped as a real god (akua mooli). [...] After this, [his] bones continued to be a god demanding worship, and such a deity was called an aumakua. Common people were sometimes deified, but not in the same manner as were kings." [818] This quote comes from a very old source--originally written in 1898--but it is one of a handful of native, nineteenth-century sources that is often quoted by more recent scholarship as relatively trustworthy. NB This descriptive text refers to the period following European contact. The fact that certain elements of the ritual-religious system the Europeans encountered in Hawaii had probably been in place for a few centuries at first seemed sufficient to infer the presence of other elements, such as this one. However, this approach now seems too speculative.

♠ Automatic deification ♣ suspected unknown ♥ "The kuni priest took a part of the flesh of the dead king's body to be used as maunu in their incantations against those who had prayed him to death. [...] After this was accomplished, the bones were put in position and arranged to resemble the shape of a man, being seated in the house until the day of prayer, when their deification would take place and they would be addressed in prayer by the kahuna of the mua. However, this approach now seems too speculative.

♠ Rulers are gods ♣ suspected unknown ♥ "Clearly, the highest ranks of alii shared a number of attributes with the gods, and indeed were explicitly called alii akua or 'god-kings' (Valeri 1985a:143; Malo 1951:54; Kamakau 1961:4). Valeri (1985a: 142-53) discusses the ethnographic evidence for divine kingship in late Hawai'i, and I will reiterate only a few key points. As the ritual leader of his people, who intercedes with the gods to assure the flow of mana, the king 'instantiates different major gods or groups of their particularizations according to a precise ritual calendar' (1985a:142). Thus when he goes to war, the king offers a human sacrifice at the luakini temple of Ku, but at other times he consecrates temples to the agricultural gods Lono and Kāne. The king shares with these gods the right to the kapu moe, the prostrating kapu. Another sign of their divinity is the capacity of high-ranked alii to practice incestuous marriage, something that the gods provide the model for (as in the creation myth of Wākea and Papa, Beckwith [1940]). Gods and kings share other material insignia as well: the puloulou staff, and the wrapping of their bodies in feathered garments (see below). Holding the power of life or death over ordinary humans, the king is frequently described as the ultimate devourer, a shark (in particular, the tiger shark, Galeocerdo cuvieri). [...] By the time of initial contact with Europeans, Hawaiians had taken the older Polynesian concepts of chiefship and rank, and subjected them to a form of hypertrophy, the logical extension of which was that their rulers, their kings, were now held to be divine." [820] NB This descriptive text refers to the period following European contact. The fact that certain elements of the ritual-religious system the Europeans encountered in Hawaii had probably been in place for a few centuries at first seemed sufficient to infer the presence of other elements, such as this one. However, this approach now seems too speculative.

Normative Ideological Aspects of Equity and Prosociality[edit]

♠ Ideological reinforcement of equality ♣ suspected unknown ♥ "Kapu is often glossed as either 'sacred,' or 'prohibited,' and refers tothat state which is necessary for the protection of mana. Its opposite is noa, 'free' from kapu, hence sometimes glossed as 'profane.' Thesesimple glosses mask a tangled set of relationships that bind the three terms into a complex whole. Recall that Kepelino used noa as the term for the class of common people; they were noa in relation to the alii, who were kapu, because the latter were intermediaries between the gods and the society at large, the all-important transmitters of mana." [821] NB This descriptive text refers to the period following European contact. The fact that certain elements of the ritual-religious system the Europeans encountered in Hawaii had probably been in place for a few centuries at first seemed sufficient to infer the presence of other elements, such as this one. However, this approach now seems too speculative.

♠ Ideological thought equates elites and commoners ♣ suspected unknown ♥ "In short, late precontact Hawaiian society segmented itself into three lexically marked social classes. These were ranked relative to one another, not only according to a system of hierarchical privileges and access to resources, but in relation to a deeply ingrained ideological system (the kapu system) which underpinned the annual cycle of religious practices." [823] "Kapu is often glossed as either 'sacred,' or 'prohibited,' and refers tothat state which is necessary for the protection of mana. However, this approach now seems too speculative.
♠ Ideological thought equates all ethnicities ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Ideological thought equates men and women ♣ suspected unknown ♥ "What is sometimes referred to as the 'kapu system' was in reality a whole series of socially and ritually prescribed practices that controlled the daily lives and bodily practices of all ranks of late Hawaiian society. At its core was the ̒ai kapu, the 'eating taboo' that controlled gender relations, and specified that men and women could not eat together, that their foods must be cooked in separate ovens, and that certain foods (pork, in particular, but also certain kinds of bananas, fish, and so on) were prohibited to women." [825] "Along with these positive attributes, women--and therefore goddesses--have negative ones, springing from the fact that they are considered impure (see part 2). Beckwith even believed she had established a relation between the name Haumea and the word haumia, which means 'impure' and refers, among other things, to the state of women during menstruation (1940, 289). Female impurity explains women's close connection with sorcery." [826] NB This descriptive text refers to the period following European contact. The fact that certain elements of the ritual-religious system the Europeans encountered in Hawaii had probably been in place for a few centuries at first seemed sufficient to infer the presence of other elements, such as this one. However, this approach now seems too speculative.

♠ Ideological reinforcement of inequality ♣ present♥ "Kapu is often glossed as either 'sacred,' or 'prohibited,' and refers to that state which is necessary for the protection of mana. ♠ Ideological reinforcement of monogamy ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Kings had to observe a number of restrictions in order to safeguard their mana, and led lives that are adhered closely to the kapu system. Royal marriage, too, was "highly prescribed" [833]. However, kings were allowed to take multiple wives--as long as a distinction was maintained between wives of commoner origins and ones that belonged to the elite. The fact that they were allowed to take multiple wives suggests that monogamy was not in any way enforced or encouraged: "In principle, if not wholly in practice, the classes were endogamous; the major exceptions consisted of the appropriation of exceptionally beautiful young women of the commoner class by elite males, but only as secondary wives or concubines. The primary wives of the elites, and especially the royal wives of the king, were carefully chosen from among the alii class." [834] The following quote is relevant and interesting, but it is worth noting that the author was a nineteenth-century Christian Hawaiian who sometimes attempted to square Hawaiian traditions with Christian ones: "The marriage (mare) called ho'ao pa'a was an ancient custom by which a man and a woman were bound in a lasting union (ua pa'a loa ka noho ana), a man not to desert his wife nor a woman he husband. This form of marriage, in which each took a single mate, originated as a command from the god to Hulihonua and his wife Keakahulilani, and lasted from their time down to that of Kahiko Luamea, 27 generations later. It was Wakea ['son' of Kahiko Luamea] who introduced the 'sin' (hewa) of mating with many women (moe lehulehu). Wakea took three wives, and his wife Papa in revenge took eight new husbands: Lua, Makea, Waia, Hinanalo, Kakahili, Wailoa, and a couple of others. After Wakea's time the unions of succeeding generations divided into two kinds, those in which men took many wives and women many husbands, and those in which there were but one wife or one husband. Formerly, the latter were in the majority. It was the chiefs and wealthy people who took more than one wife or husband." [835] NB This descriptive text refers to the period following European contact. The fact that certain elements of the ritual-religious system the Europeans encountered in Hawaii had probably been in place for a few centuries at first seemed sufficient to infer the presence of other elements, such as this one. However, this approach now seems too speculative.

♠ Ideology reinforces prosociality ♣ suspected unknown ♥

♠ charity ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ production of public goods ♣ suspected unknown ♥ absent/present/unknown. Public Goods refer to anything that incurs cost to an individual or group of individuals, but that can be used or enjoyed by others who did not incur any of the cost, namely the public at large. They are non-excludable and non-rivalrous goods. Examples are roads, public drinking fountains, public parks or theatres, temples open to the public, etc. NB This descriptive text refers to the period following European contact. The fact that certain elements of the ritual-religious system the Europeans encountered in Hawaii had probably been in place for a few centuries at first seemed sufficient to infer the presence of other elements, such as this one. However, this approach now seems too speculative.
♠ communal dining ♣ suspected unknown ♥ "On the evening of the twenty-third day [of the year and of the Makahiki cycle], after the god is decorated, everyone gets dressed in his best. Nobles and commoners chant aloud prayer called kauwō (K. Kamaku 1919-20, 41; cf. PE, 129). Pigs are cooked in the oven for the men and dogs for the women. [...] For four days one only has to eat well and have a good time" [836]. NB This descriptive text refers to the period following European contact. The fact that certain elements of the ritual-religious system the Europeans encountered in Hawaii had probably been in place for a few centuries at first seemed sufficient to infer the presence of other elements, such as this one. However, this approach now seems too speculative.
♠ morality ♣ suspected unknown ♥ "What is sometimes referred to as the 'kapu system' was in reality a whole series of socially and ritually prescribed practices that controlled the daily lives and bodily practices of all ranks of late Hawaiian society." [837] "4. Coveting the property of another has many aspects to it, a spying upon another, lying in ambush on his trail, plotting, treachery, deceit, trickery with the intent to murder secretly in order to get someone's goods. All of these things come under the head of robbery and are of the nature of murder (pepehi wale).//5. If one has determined to enrich at another's expense the evil has many shapes. [...]//6. If a man wishes to deal truthfully with another and afterwards finds that things have been misrepresented to him, there are many things involved in that. [...]//10. There were all sins, clearly understood to be wrong[...].//16. There was a large number of actions that were considered essentially good (pono maoli)[...].//17. To act justly without trespassing or deceiving, not frequenting another's house, not gazing wistfully upon your neighbor's goods not begging for anything that belongs to him--that is the prudent course.//18. The following actions were considered worthy of approbation: to live thriftily, not to be a vagabond, not to keep changing wives, not to be always shifting from one chief to another, not to run in debt." [838] This quote comes from a very old source but it is one of a handful of native, nineteenth-century sources that is often quoted by more recent scholarship as relatively trustworthy. NB This descriptive text refers to the period following European contact. The fact that certain elements of the ritual-religious system the Europeans encountered in Hawaii had probably been in place for a few centuries at first seemed sufficient to infer the presence of other elements, such as this one. However, this approach now seems too speculative.

Normative Ideological Aspects of Slavery[edit]

♠ Ideology forbids slavery for co-ethnics ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Slaves in a Western sense did not exist in Hawaiian society at this time. "The papa kauwā, at the bottom of the social scale, are sometimes translated in Western literature as 'slaves,' but a better term is probably 'outcast.' Malo 1951:68-71) explains that kauwā was a general term of degradation that was widely used in a metaphoric sense, such as a younger brother referring to himself as the kauwā of his older (higher ranked) brother, or a retainer calling himself the kauwā of his chief. True kauwā, however, were a special category of persons (often physically marked by special tattoos on their faces) who could be taken for the purpose of human sacrifice at war temples." [839] NB This descriptive text refers to the period following European contact. The fact that certain elements of the ritual-religious system the Europeans encountered in Hawaii had probably been in place for a few centuries at first seemed sufficient to infer the presence of other elements, such as this one. However, this approach now seems too speculative.
♠ Ideology forbids slavery for co-religionists ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Slaves in a Western sense did not exist in Hawaiian society at this time. Malo 1951:68-71) explains that kauwā was a general term of degradation that was widely used in a metaphoric sense, such as a younger brother referring to himself as the kauwā of his older (higher ranked) brother, or a retainer calling himself the kauwā of his chief. True kauwā, however, were a special category of persons (often physically marked by special tattoos on their faces) who could be taken for the purpose of human sacrifice at war temples." [840] NB This descriptive text refers to the period following European contact. The fact that certain elements of the ritual-religious system the Europeans encountered in Hawaii had probably been in place for a few centuries at first seemed sufficient to infer the presence of other elements, such as this one. However, this approach now seems too speculative.
♠ Ideology forbids slavery universally ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Slaves in a Western sense did not exist in Hawaiian society at this time. "The papa kauwā, at the bottom of the social scale, are sometimes translated in Western literature as 'slaves,' but a better term is probably 'outcast.' Malo 1951:68-71) explains that kauwā was a general term of degradation that was widely used in a metaphoric sense, such as a younger brother referring to himself as the kauwā of his older (higher ranked) brother, or a retainer calling himself the kauwā of his chief. True kauwā, however, were a special category of persons (often physically marked by special tattoos on their faces) who could be taken for the purpose of human sacrifice at war temples." [841] NB This descriptive text refers to the period following European contact. The fact that certain elements of the ritual-religious system the Europeans encountered in Hawaii had probably been in place for a few centuries at first seemed sufficient to infer the presence of other elements, such as this one. However, this approach now seems too speculative.

Normative Ideological precepts concerning morality/supernatural beings[edit]

Supernatural beings (e.g., gods, spirits, ancestors)

♠ Number of officially sanctioned supernatural beings ♣ suspected unknown ♥ "The four 'major gods' are called po' o ki' eki' e, 'high heads' (Kepelino 1932, 19) or, collectively, ke koko' oha o ke akua, 'the association of four gods' (Fornander 191-20, 4: 605). [...] It should be noted that the totality of deities is defined by the expression - often placed at the beginning of prayers (cf. Beckwith 1940, 82) - i kini o ke akua, ka lehu o ke akua, ka mano o ke akua, 'the 40,000 deities, the 400,000 deities, the 4,000 deities' (Emerson 1965, 21). [...] The relationship between the major and minor gods is presented in a somewhat confused and contradictory manner in the literature (Emerson 1892, 5-24; Handy and Pukui 1972, 117-31; NK, 1: 35-43; Kamakau 1961, 200). According to one Hawaiian author the relationship is identical to that of the eldest brother and his younger brothers; the first reigns over the group, and the younger ones rule over special domains assigned to them (kuleana) (Anonymous 1927, 76). For Kamakau, on the contrary, subordinate gods are produced by a 'segmentation' (mahae'ana) of the major gods."[856] NB This descriptive text refers to the period following European contact. The fact that certain elements of the ritual-religious system the Europeans encountered in Hawaii had probably been in place for a few centuries at first seemed sufficient to infer the presence of other elements, such as this one. However, this approach now seems too speculative.
♠ Omnipresent supernatural beings ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Omniscient supernatural beings ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Omnipotent supernatural beings ♣ suspected unknown ♥ There are several legends[857] in which the gods are shown to be, at the very least, "active", in the sense that they do intervene in human affairs, but may not always be motivate to enforce morality. NB This descriptive text refers to the period following European contact. The fact that certain elements of the ritual-religious system the Europeans encountered in Hawaii had probably been in place for a few centuries at first seemed sufficient to infer the presence of other elements, such as this one. However, this approach now seems too speculative.
♠ High Gods (creator gods) ♣ suspected unknown ♥ A creator god existed, and his name was Kane. However, it is not clear that he also enforced moral behaviour among humans. But, based on the fact that people prayed to him in temples, asking him to grant them things like wisdom and prosperity, perhaps it is reasonable to code him as "active". Admittedly, however, things are somewhat complicated by the fact that Kane was also assisted by the gods Ku and Lono during creation (though he himself played the dominant role), and that, when people prayed to Kane, they often prayed "through" lesser gods, e.g. family gods ('aumakua). Based on information provided by Beckwith[858], though we also made sure that our codings do not diverge from the supplementary materials in Watts et al. (2015)[859] NB This descriptive text refers to the period following European contact. The fact that certain elements of the ritual-religious system the Europeans encountered in Hawaii had probably been in place for a few centuries at first seemed sufficient to infer the presence of other elements, such as this one. However, this approach now seems too speculative.

Supernatural Enforcement of Morality

For this section, include any type of supernatural punishment or reward either in this lifetime (e.g., curses, good fortune) or in the afterlife (e.g., hell, reincarnation). This includes generalized impersonal punishment/reward (e.g., karma, mana) as well as punishment by specific supernatural beings. Include specific details of the nature and extent of punishment/reward in the description. Code “compound” moral actions (e.g., filial piety = kin + respect) in multiple categories as necessary. Morality categories are based on Moral Foundations Theory (Haidt & Graham, 2007) and Morality-As-Cooperation theory (Curry, 2016).
♠ supernatural enforcement against harm ♣ suspected unknown ♥ "The bond between humans and their personal, ancestral gods was very real. By the age of seven a child was expected to say his own prayers to his 'aumakua. His family and his 'aumakua expected him to refrain from breaking the laws of the many gods, do observe and obey the kapua pronounced by the chiefs and kahuna and to be a helpful person and refrain from hurting his fellow men in any way."[860] NB This descriptive text refers to the period following European contact. The fact that certain elements of the ritual-religious system the Europeans encountered in Hawaii had probably been in place for a few centuries at first seemed sufficient to infer the presence of other elements, such as this one. However, this approach now seems too speculative.

Human Enforcement of Morality

♠ care/harm ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ fairness/cheating ♣ suspected unknown ♥ "If a man wishes to deal truthfully with another and afterwards finds that things have been misrepresented to him, there are many things involved in that. In the first place there is deceit (hoo-punipuni), lying (waha-hee(, slabder (alapahi), falsehood (palau), the lie jestful (ku-kahe-kahe), the lie fluent (palolo), the lie unclothed (kokahe), the lie direct (pahilau), and many other things of like sort. [...] There were all sins, clearly understood to be wrong[...]." [869] This quote comes from a very old source but it is one of a handful of native, nineteenth-century sources that is often quoted by more recent scholarship as relatively trustworthy. However, it presents no evidence that fairness was rewarded or cheating punished.
♠ in-group loyalty ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ respect for authority ♣ suspected unknown ♥ "As indicated in Table 2.2, the two highest ranks of alii, the niaupio and pio, were due a particular form of deference known as the kapu moe, the 'prostrating taboo.' This required everyone immediately to strip off any upper body garments, and lie prostrate on the ground until the chief had passed out of view. Anyone failing to perform the kapu moe was subject to immediate death. The only persons exempt from the kapu moe were alii of the next two categories (naha and wohi chiefs), who nonetheless were required to sit in the presence of the niaupio and pio alii." [870] NB This descriptive text refers to the period following European contact. The fact that certain elements of the ritual-religious system the Europeans encountered in Hawaii had probably been in place for a few centuries at first seemed sufficient to infer the presence of other elements, such as this one. However, this approach now seems too speculative.
♠ purity ♣ suspected unknown ♥ "Although our information is often scanty on this point, it appears that Hawaiians classify a variety of states as 'pure' and 'impure'. [...] The most impure bodily process is menstruation. [...] Along with menstrual blood, all shed blood is impure to different degrees. [...] Loss of grease (which is apparently believed to embody life) also produces pollution (Kamaku 1964, 37). [...] Also impure is anything organic that putrefies, such as corpses or excrement--and everybody who comes in contact with these things or even worships them [...] Finally, aborted fetuses, deformed individuals, dwarfs and cretings are also impure and polluting (Stewart 1838, 191; cf. Ii 1963, 101).//All these are impure because they manifest a loss of integrity, of life (examples: loss of blood or of grease, corruption) or the nonachievement of a normal form, of a perfectly functioning life (examples: aborted fetuses, monsters). These phenomena do not simply imply a contrast with the divine type that they should instantiate; they also involve a contrast with the group constituted by the people who correctly instantiate that type. Any lack of bodily integrity therefore entails a lack of social integrity, a state of disorder in the relationship between afflicted person and group. Thus a dead person or a menstruating woman produces disorder, a weakness, in the group that must be resolved by purification." [871] "The kapu enveloping the most sacred Hawaiian chiefs and kings were elaborated and intensified to a greater degree than elsewhere in Polynesia (except, perhaps, Tonga). Their premises, their clothing, their food, and their bodies were all subject to and regulated by a complex set of prescriptions and prohibitions, designed to protect their mana and keep them from contact with polluting influences (noa)." [872] NB This descriptive text refers to the period following European contact. The fact that certain elements of the ritual-religious system the Europeans encountered in Hawaii had probably been in place for a few centuries at first seemed sufficient to infer the presence of other elements, such as this one. However, this approach now seems too speculative.
♠ kinship ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ bravery ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ respect for property ♣ suspected unknown ♥ "There were two principal sins (hewa nui) for which punishment was applied while a person lived, and after he died. One was for stealing, 'aihue [...]. The punishment of a thief when he was caught stealing by the owner of the property was a stern one--his flesh was stripped off his bones (holehole) until he died. This is a saying of the people of old, 'A thief denies his guilt until his bones are stripped of flesh,' I ka ho'ole no ka 'aihue a pau na iwi i ka holehole. [...] For these sins there was bitter punishment and cruelty before death, and the bones of their perpetrators could not be hidden away. In ancient times other sins could be absolved by the gods, and perhaps it was thought that the sin of [outright] murder was less important than these because it was so rarely committed." [873] This quote comes from a very old source but it is one of a handful of native, nineteenth-century sources that is often quoted by more recent scholarship as relatively trustworthy. NB This descriptive text refers to the period following European contact. The fact that certain elements of the ritual-religious system the Europeans encountered in Hawaii had probably been in place for a few centuries at first seemed sufficient to infer the presence of other elements, such as this one. However, this approach now seems too speculative.

♠ Moralistic punishment by human religious agents ♣ suspected unknown ♥ This variable codes whether or not religious agents or religiously motivated individuals could punish moral transgressors independently of the state. Though moral transgressions were indeed punishable by death, this did not occur independently of the state: "In contact-era Hawai'i, the legitimate use of force included not only the ability of kings to wage wars of territorial conquest, but also to exercise the right over life or death in the everyday affairs of the common people. This kind of continual threat no doubt was highly effective in maintaining alii control. The frequent references in the ethnohistoric sources to death as a penalty for breaking some kapu are striking. Failure to perform the prostrating kapu in the presence of a high-ranking alii, any number of violations against the person or property of the king of a chief, coughing or uttering any noise during a religious service, and various other infractions were all punishable by death." [874] NB This descriptive text refers to the period following European contact. The fact that certain elements of the ritual-religious system the Europeans encountered in Hawaii had probably been in place for a few centuries at first seemed sufficient to infer the presence of other elements, such as this one. However, this approach now seems too speculative.

Failure to perform the prostrating kapu in the presence of a high-ranking alii, any number of violations against the person or property of the king of a chief, coughing or uttering any noise during a religious service, and various other infractions were all punishable by death." [880] NB This descriptive text refers to the period following European contact. The fact that certain elements of the ritual-religious system the Europeans encountered in Hawaii had probably been in place for a few centuries at first seemed sufficient to infer the presence of other elements, such as this one. However, this approach now seems too speculative.
♠ punishment of commoners ♣ suspected unknown ♥ This variable codes whether or not religious agents or religiously motivated individuals could punish moral transgressors independently of the state. Though moral transgressions were indeed punishable by death, this did not occur independently of the state: "In contact-era Hawai'i, the legitimate use of force included not only the ability of kings to wage wars of territorial conquest, but also to exercise the right over life or death in the everyday affairs of the common people. This kind of continual threat no doubt was highly effective in maintaining alii control. The frequent references in the ethnohistoric sources to death as a penalty for breaking some kapu are striking. Failure to perform the prostrating kapu in the presence of a high-ranking alii, any number of violations against the person or property of the king of a chief, coughing or uttering any noise during a religious service, and various other infractions were all punishable by death." [881] NB This descriptive text refers to the period following European contact. The fact that certain elements of the ritual-religious system the Europeans encountered in Hawaii had probably been in place for a few centuries at first seemed sufficient to infer the presence of other elements, such as this one. However, this approach now seems too speculative.

Well-Being[edit]

Economic Well-Being[edit]

State Intervention in Economy

♠ The state regulates banking ♣ inferred absent ♥ No banking in Hawai'i at this time: the first bank in the islands was opened by American businessman Charles Reed Bishop in 1848.[882]
♠ Price controls ♣ ♥
