♠ Standardization of weights/measures ♣ ♥
♠ Guarantee stability of coin value ♣ ♥
♠ Public markets ♣ absent ♥ Hommon has argued that pre-contact Hawaiian society was marked by 'the absence or minimal development' of various features, including markets.[883]

Contracts

♠ State enforcement ♣ ♥
♠ Non-state enforcement ♣ ♥
♠ Corporate Agency ♣ ♥
♠ State contracting ♣ ♥
♠ Limited Liability ♣ ♥

Patronage

♠ within-class formal patronage ♣ ♥
♠ between-class formal patronage ♣ present ♥ 'Hawaiian elites supported the production of art objects through the patronage of craft specialists who often resided in, or adjacent to, the royal households'.[884] For instance, Kirch mentions the 'royal patronage of canoe builders, an expert guild known as kahuna kālai wa'a.'[885] The fact that a 'guild' could be supported over multiple generations, and more generally the high degree of skill of Hawaiian craft specialists, suggest that these were formalized relationships of patronage.
♠ within-class informal patronage ♣ ♥
♠ between-class informal patronage ♣ ♥

Public Goods[edit]

♠ Famine relief ♣ {absent; present} ♥ Sahlins and Kirch seem to agree that chiefs would sometimes, or at least were expected to, provide food to commoners in the event of famine.[886][887] However, Kirch wrote in his 2010 book that food storage played only a limited role in the precontact and contact-era Hawaiian economy: the 'primary root, tuber, and tree crops that formed the Hawaiian subsistence base were not generally amenable to storage'.[888] It therefore seems unlikely that there would have been any large-scale distribution of food to commoners in times of famine. 'Little is known' of famine in precontact Hawai'i, but Kamakau mentions an early example from Maui, during which the people subsisted on various weeds. Schmitt comments that 'such an emergency diet was not uncommon', suggesting a limited role for famine relief provided by the state or religious institutions.[889] The mixed dryland cultivation that dominated the agriculture of the Big Island (except for the Kohala area) was more vulnerable to famine than the irrigated taro cultivation common on the older, western islands such as O'ahu and Kaua'i.[890]

♠ provision of famine relief ♣ ♥

♠ Food storage ♣ {absent; present} ♥ High status individuals (chiefs, etc.) had houses for the storage of provisions as part of their household clusters.[891] Food storage sheds were called hale papa'a. All scholars agree that these provisions were intended for redistribution, but it is unclear to whom. Sahlins implies that the food was redistributed to the people, including commoners.[892] But Kirch states that the food was redistributed almost entirely to other chiefs, lesser chiefs, retainers, etc., with only token amounts, at most, going to commoners.[893] He also states that food storage was difficult given the climate and kinds of crops that Hawaiians cultivated, so chiefs had to physically travel to different areas of their chiefdoms in order to exact tribute, rather than being able to store all of the tribute in a central location.[894] Meanwhile, Valeri states that none of the tribute was redistributed to the commoners.[895] Sahlins and Kirch seem to agree that chiefs would sometimes, or at least were expected to, provide food to commoners in the event of famine.[896][897]

♠ provision of food storage ♣ ♥

♠ Alimentary supplementation ♣ {absent; present} ♥ High status individuals (chiefs, etc.) had houses for the storage of provisions as part of their household clusters.[898] Food storage sheds were called hale papa'a. All scholars agree that these provisions were intended for redistribution, but it is unclear to whom. Sahlins implies that the food was redistributed to the people, including commoners.[899] But Kirch states that the food was redistributed almost entirely to other chiefs, lesser chiefs, retainers, etc., with only token amounts, at most, going to commoners.[900] He also states that food storage was difficult given the climate and kinds of crops that Hawaiians cultivated, so chiefs had to physically travel to different areas of their chiefdoms in order to exact tribute, rather than being able to store all of the tribute in a central location.[901] Meanwhile, Valeri states that none of the tribute was redistributed to the commoners.[902] Sahlins and Kirch seem to agree that chiefs would sometimes, or at least were expected to, provide food to commoners in the event of famine.[903][904]

♠ provision of alimentary supplementation ♣ ♥

♠ Drinking water ♣ absent ♥ According to Schmitt, the Hawaiians before European contact did not construct drinking water supply systems but relied on natural sources: 'Even the most rudimentary plumbing was unknown before Captain Cook's arrival. The ancient Hawaiians depended on streams and springs for their water supply, sometimes carrying calabashes of water great distances over rugged terrain'.[905]

♠ provision of drinking water ♣ ♥

♠ Irrigation works ♣ present ♥ Irrigation infrastructure was extensive, including water-diversion walls for dryland agriculture[906] and pondfields for taro.[907] On the Big Island, true irrigated agriculture was mainly confined to the Kohala and Hāmākua valleys.[908] 'As Earle (1978, 1980) argues, the size and complexity of Hawaiian irrigation did not require elaborate management; most systems could have been constructed and maintained by small social groups. Nevertheless, once constructed, irrigation systems produced high yields for labor invested. This advantage was not lost on the Hawaiian chiefs, who seized every opportunity to expand the irrigation systems under their control, and who rewarded loyal supporters with grants of prime irrigation land'.[909] In the Native Hawaiian conception, 'Water ... like sunlight, as a source of life to land and man, was the possession of no man, even ali'i [chiefs] or the mo'i [ruler]'.[910]

♠ provision of irrigation works ♣ mixed ♥ It appears that both non-state (community) and state agents contributed to the provision of irrigation works. Some infrastructure was probably constructed and worked by groups of commoners, with various different ali'i claiming taxation rights over the crops produced in the fields.[911] Handy and Handy take a slightly different view, arguing that 'The building and maintenance of flooded terraces (lo'i) and of the irrigation ditches ('auwai) were communal procedures. This type of work would certainly never have been achieved had the old Hawaiians done their farming on an individualistic basis, without the planning and direction of proprietary chiefs (ali'i)'.[912] Overall, I think we can say that Hawaiian chiefs took advantage of their corvée rights to expand existing irrigation infrastructure built by commoners.[913]

♠ Health infrastructure ♣ present ♥ According to Samuel Kamakau, there were eight categories of professional kahuna lapa'au, priests with medicinal roles. They included priests who could deliver babies, diagnose dislocations and sprains, and use magic to cure patients.[914]

♠ Control of infectious disease ♣ ♥
♠ Care of war invalids/veterans ♣ present ♥ Pu'uhonua enclosures served as sanctuaries for vulnerable groups, including 'vanquished warriors escaping their pursuers', in times of war.[915] Certain portions of the ahupua'a districts were also pu'uhonua in that they were 'neutral grounds' where 'water, food, fibre, shelter, etc. could be obtained for the people who took refuge there'.[916]
♠ Care of orphans ♣ inferred present ♥ Pu'uhonua enclosures served as sanctuaries for vulnerable groups, including children, in times of war.[917] Presumably this would have extended to orphaned children. Certain portions of the ahupua'a districts were also pu'uhonua in that they were 'neutral grounds' where 'water, food, fibre, shelter, etc. could be obtained for the people who took refuge there'.[918]
♠ Care of elderly ♣ present ♥ 'In traditional Hawai'i, there were several kānāwai (edicts or laws) proclaimed by the paramount chiefs to ensure the safety of children and of the weak and elderly'.[919] More generally, pu'uhonua enclosures served as sanctuaries for the old (as well as children and the sick) in times of war.[920] Certain portions of the ahupua'a districts were also pu'uhonua in that they were 'neutral grounds' where 'water, food, fibre, shelter, etc. could be obtained for the people who took refuge there'.[921]

♠ State pensions for elites ♣ inferred absent ♥ I have found no mention in the sources of pensions in pre-contact Hawai'i.

♠ State pensions for non-elites ♣ inferred absent ♥ I have found no mention in the sources of pensions in pre-contact Hawai'i.

Entertainment and Knowledge Well-Being[edit]

♠ Funding for festivals ♣ ♥

♠ Entertainment buildings ♣ present ♥

♠ Theatres ♣ ♥
♠ Arenas ♣ inferred present ♥ We know that arenas were present in the succeeding period, USKameh: 'John Papa 'Ī'ī, who from the age of about 10 years served in the royal court of Kamehameha ... describes the time devoted by members of the royal household to such sports as maika stone bowling (special maika courts were adjacent to the royal compound)'.[922] Maika was also played during Hawaii3, so presumably arenas must also have been present.[923]
♠ Ritual buildings/spaces ♣ present ♥ In a general discussion of precontact Hawaiian society, Kirch writes: 'Temples rose upon massive stone platforms that evinced the power and authority of ruling chiefs, and served as constant reminders of the role in the life of the community of an extensive pantheon'.[924]
♠ Other ♣ ♥
♠ accessibility of entertainment buildings ♣ ♥

♠ Full-time entertainment specialists ♣ inferred present ♥ Storytellers formed part of the extensive retinue attached to any powerful chief's (or king's) household.[925]

♠ status of full-time entertainment specialists ♣ ♥

♠ Access to education ♣ all people ♥ Hula dance performances, 'witnessed and enjoyed by people of all ranks' and performed by both men and women, were occasions for the recitation of Hawaii's extensive oral literature, which 'consisted of meles (songs) of various kinds, genealogies and honorific chants, stories and traditional lore in which were imbedded fragments of history and biography'.[926] N. B. Emerson, historian of the hula and son of a Protestant missionary to Hawai'i, wrote that for ancient Hawaiians, the hula took the 'place of our [Westerners'] concert-hall and lecture-room, our opera and theater'.[927]

♠ Publically funded or subsidized education ♣ ♥
♠ Schools for children? ♣ ♥
♠ Universities, “proto-universities”, or other higher education ♣ ♥
♠ Libraries ♣ inferred absent ♥ The pre-contact Hawaiians had no writing.[928]
♠ Museums ♣ ♥
♠ Literacy rate ♣ 0 ♥ The pre-contact Hawaiians had no writing.[929]

Biological Well-Being[edit]

Mortality

♠ Life expectancy ♣ ♥ Kirch gives average life expectancies for men (32 years) and women (29 years), but notes that this figure is 'skewed by a fairly high rate of infant mortality'.[930]
♠ Infant mortality ♣ ♥ The infant mortality rate was 'fairly high' according to Kirch.[931]

Disease

♠ Endemic disease ♣ inferred absent ♥ Certain pathologies were apparently 'common': 'chronic arthritis (both osteoarthritis and rheumatoid arthritis) and osteophytosis', but other comments by Kirch and C. E. Snow on the general 'good health' and 'extraordinary muscular development' of the early Hawaiians imply that we cannot say that 'Fatal or debilitating disease is common or widespread' (the description in the code book).[932][933]
♠ Disease outbreaks ♣ ♥
♠ Endemic malnutrition ♣ inferred absent ♥ Skeletal evidence reveals that '[i]n general, the early Hawaiians enjoyed good health'.[934] After osteological analysis of 1,171 burials from O'ahu, C. E. Snow wrote that 'The limb and hip bones showed an extraordinary muscular development, in women as well as men. Indeed, all of their bones bespeak the vigorous and strenuous outdoor existence of these people'.[935] Early European visitors (e.g. George Dixon) also commented on the 'general good health and physique' of the population,[936] so it seems unlikely that malnutrition was endemic.

Physiology

♠ Average height (male) ♣ [170-173] ♥ metres. From osteological analysis of human remains, C. E. Snow concluded that 'the Hawaiians were a physically homogeneous population ... In height, the average man stood about 5 feet 7 inches [1.7 m], while the average woman was a bit shorter, 5 feet 3 inches [1.6 m]'.[937] Another estimate: 1400-1800 CE skeletal remains averaged 173 cm.[938]
♠ Average height (female) ♣ 160 ♥ metres. From osteological analysis of human remains, C. E. Snow concluded that 'the Hawaiians were a physically homogeneous population ... In height, the average man stood about 5 feet 7 inches [1.7 m], while the average woman was a bit shorter, 5 feet 3 inches [1.6 m]'.[939]

Prosocial Behavior[edit]

♠ Volunteering ♣ ♥

♠ Charity ♣ inferred present ♥ In Matsuda's description of pre-contact Hawaiian communities, 'It was considered shameful ... to fail to share with those in need'.[940] Traditionally, much intracommunity exchange took the form of reciprocal gift-giving (rather than 'barter', the direct exchange of one item for another of equivalent value): 'In any traditional Polynesian society ... the ubiquitous reciprocal giving of food and other gifts enhances social interaction between families and other groups'.[941]

Voluntary Associations

♠ voluntary trade associations ♣ ♥
♠ prestige of voluntary trade associations ♣ ♥
♠ voluntary community organizations ♣ ♥
♠ prestige of voluntary community organizations ♣ ♥
♠ voluntary athletic clubs ♣ ♥ Sports were very popular among both chiefs and commoners: during the yearly Makahiki celebrations, competitive tournaments were witnessed by thousands of people.[942] However, it's not clear from the sources whether there were voluntary athletic clubs.
♠ prestige of voluntary athletic clubs organizations ♣ ♥
♠ other voluntary organizations ♣ ♥
♠ prestige of other voluntary organizations ♣ ♥
♠ voluntary religious associations ♣ inferred present ♥ Alongside the 'official priests' who performed rituals, there were priests (or 'shamans') known as kaula in Hawai'i at this time (taura elsewhere in Polynesia). They have been described as 'female and male inspired priests who acted as healers, oracles, and mediums, and they were quite distinct from another class of priests known as tofunga (kahuna in Hawaiian), who were specialists in traditions, ritual formulae, and an array of ritualized craft activities'.[943] 'Shamanism was sometimes inherited but more frequently arose from possession, from an act of selection attributed to a deity'.[944] The female priesthood of Pele, the volcano goddess, could also possibly count as a voluntary religious association, existing in parallel to the state cult. Goldman writes that '[m]en and women were separated because they came under the jurisdiction of different classes of gods ... Insofar as they came under the jurisdiction of their own class of deities, among them Pele, the goddess of fire and volcanoes, women had access to mana and came under their own order of sanctity. The female order was, however, subordinate'.[945]
♠ prestige of voluntary religious associations ♣ lower ♥ In Hawai'i, kaula (glossed as 'shamans' by Thomas) seem to have had a 'marginal' status: '[T]he only parts of the Hawaiian archipelago in which shamans were important were socially peripheral places'.[946]

Economy variables (polity-level)[edit]

♠ Labor ♣ ♥
♠ Currency ♣ inferred absent ♥ 'Needless to say, there was no money (in Diamond's words, no "abstract, intrinsically valueless medium for appropriating surplus, storing value, and deferring payment or delaying exchange") in precontact Hawai'i'.[947]

Debt and Indemnities

♠ Polity has obligation to other polity or group ♣ ♥
♠ Polity receives obligatory payment from other polity or group ♣ ♥

Taxation[edit]

♠ Degree of taxation ♣ ♥

Taxes are imposed on:

♠ Land ♣ present ♥ During tax collections associated with the yearly Makahiki festival, 'In general, Malo relates ... the size of the tax levy was proportional to the size of the land division'.[948] Referring to more everyday tax collections, Beechert also writes: 'The individual farmer paid his share of the group tax through his group's headman, the haku. The individual tax was levied in proportion to the land cultivated'.[949] In each ahupua'a there were also parcels of land set aside called kō'ele: they were worked by the commoners but all produce from them went to the chief in charge of the ahupua'a.[950]
♠ Property ♣ ♥
♠ People ♣ ♥
♠ Trade ♣ ♥
♠ Income ♣ ♥
♠ Production ♣ present ♥ The taxes collected during the Makahiki and other ceremonial events included non-agricultural goods produced by commoners, such as barkcloth, pandanus mats, nets, cord and the feathers of forest birds.[951]

Taxes are imposed by:

♠ executive or ruling elite ♣ present ♥ By the time of contact with the West, the Makahiki festival had become 'the principal ritualized form of tribute exactation', used by the 'king and his subsidiary ali'i ... to collect their respective ho'okupu or tribute'.[952] For our purposes, 'tribute' as used here actually describes taxation of the population within the polity.
♠ local elites ♣ present ♥ Local chiefs and konohiki land managers both collected tribute from the commoner population.[953]
♠ state officials ♣ present ♥ By the time of contact with the West, the Makahiki festival had become 'the principal ritualized form of tribute exactation', used by the 'king and his subsidiary ali'i ... to collect their respective ho'okupu or tribute'.[954] For our purposes, 'tribute' as used here actually describes taxation of the population within the polity. The lesser ali'i mentioned here fulfilled both the position of 'state official' and 'local elite'.

Type of taxes paid:

♠ Staple ♣ present ♥ The tribute paid by commoners to elites included 'subsistence products' (as well as a range of material goods, including barkcloth and feathers).[955]
♠ Labor ♣ present ♥ In Late Hawaiian society, 'Elites held title to the land but did not work it; commoners worked the land and supported the elites through obligatory payments of tribute and labor'.[956]
♠ Currency ♣ inferred absent ♥ 'Needless to say, there was no money (in Diamond's words, no "abstract, intrinsically valueless medium for appropriating surplus, storing value, and deferring payment or delaying exchange") in precontact Hawai'i'.[957]

Wages and Costs[edit]

♠ Wages ♣ ♥ The Hawaiian economy before European contact was not structured around wage labour but formed an 'integrated system of communal activity'.[958] Beechert writes that 'there was no common medium of exchange'. Instead, commoners obtained food and tools through 'intrafamilial exchanges' and the occasional trading fair at which the surplus products of one district were exchanged for those of another.[959]

♠ staple cost of principal source of carbohydrates ♣ ♥ Not applicable: 'there was no money (in Diamond's words, no "abstract, intrinsically valueless medium for appropriating surplus, storing value, and deferring payment or delaying exchange") in precontact Hawai'i'.[960]

♠ Top fortune ♣ ♥

♠ Other wealth distribution ♣ ♥ The Makahiki rites served as a formalized way for the king and his subsidiary ali'i to extract surplus from the commoner population, involving the obligatory presentation of food and prestige goods such as barkcloth, feathers, pearls and ivory.[961] Apparently there was very little redistribution of this wealth back to the commoners: 'there can be little doubt about who benefited from the tribute collections that swept the lands for the collective waiwai, "wealth" that they could yield'.[962] (From the context here, it is clear that Kirch that means the king, ruling elite and priesthood benefited at the expense of maka'āinana.) However, more generally, Kirch argues that 'Hawai'i's high degree of stratification was achieved by the power that chiefs acquired through effective redistribution of surplus, the latter made possible by a naturally rich environment exploited through efficient technology'.[963]
